By Mark Lacter. (Will move in the ``f'' financial news file and in the ``n'' California news file.) </P> </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19940823.0205 type=multi > <HEADLINE> EXERCISE PREVENTS HEMORRHAGE IN THE ELDERLY </HEADLINE> <DATELINE>  (BC-HEALTH-BRIEFS-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> The potential benefits of regular physical exercise for older people continue to mount: reduced risk of heart attack, high blood pressure, arthritic pain, fractures from falls, among others.   Now a research team from the National Institute on Aging and the University of Iowa has found in a three-year study of more than 8,200 people 68 and older that regular moderate activity like walking or gardening can reduce the risk of severe gastrointestinal hemorrhage.   The research team, headed by Dr. Marco Pahor, who has since gone back to Rome, reported that those who took frequent walks were half as likely to develop this often-fatal condition as were inactive people of the same age.   They suggested that exercise prevented hemorrhage by improving the flow of oxygen-rich blood to the gastrointestinal tract. This, in turn, can reduce the risk of bleeding caused by insufficient oxygen under a variety of circumstances, including the body's usual reaction to stress.   Furthermore, by stimulating bowel function, exercise may reduce a person's chances of developing several intestinal disorders, including ulcers and diverticulosis, all of which increase the risk of hemorrhage.   Although only a small percentage of participants in the study regularly took part in vigorous activity, the researchers found no special benefit in preventing bleeding associated with activities more strenuous than walking. The study findings were published in Wednesday's issue of The Journal of the American Medical Association.   Smoking not only can kill but also detracts from the quality of life. A survey of more than 3,500 adults in Wisconsin found that people who smoked were more likely than nonsmokers to suffer from a variety of sleep disturbances, including difficulty falling asleep, difficulty waking up, daytime sleepiness and nightmares.   The researchers, David W. Wetter and Dr. Terry B. Young of the University of Wisconsin at Madison, suggested that smoking's effects on sleep could trap smokers in a vicious cycle, with the symptoms of sleep disturbance -- depression, fatigue and impaired ability to think, which resemble the effects of nicotine withdrawal -- possibly motivating the desire to smoke. Their findings were published last spring in the journal Preventive Medicine.   Both men and women who smoked were more likely than nonsmokers to have trouble falling asleep and waking up. Women who smoked more often found themselves plagued by daytime sleepiness, and men who smoked were more likely to have disturbing dreams, including nightmares.   The researchers suggested that various effects of smoking, including the stimulant effects of nicotine, nightly nicotine withdrawal and breathing difficulties associated with smoking, might increase the risk of sleep disturbances among smokers.   It is also possible, they wrote, that frequent sleep disturbances may increase a person's desire to smoke, since those trying to cope with the problems of nonrestorative sleep, difficulty waking up and daytime sleepiness findings, may turn to smoking as a ``remedy.''  </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19940823.0206 type=story > <HEADLINE> BONILLA GOING BACK TO WORK, BUT NOT THE KIND THAT PAYS </HEADLINE> <DATELINE>  (BC-BBO-BONILLA-600&AMP;add-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_199408.gz"

(Workman,   $15.95) For parents of children in   their second and third years.  </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950115.0014 type=other > <HEADLINE> NEW AND NOTEWORTHY PAPERBACK RELEASES </HEADLINE> <DATELINE>  (BC-PAPERBACKS-REVIEWS-$ADV22-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> THE BEGINNING OF THE JOURNEY: The Marriage of Diana and Lionel Trilling   By Diana Trilling. Harvest/Harcourt Brace, $13.95.   Married in 1929, the Trillings were at the center of New York's intellectual life in an era when the city fancied itself the center of the world. Covering the years until 1950, this is an ``extraordinary'' memoir, Ann Hulbert said here in 1993. She said Mrs. Trilling ``has miraculously discovered a morally dignified yet also ruthlessly honest style of self-exposure.''   A FROLIC OF HIS OWN   By William Gaddis. Scribner/Simon &AMP; Schuster, $12.   ``Justice? -- You get justice in the next world, in this world you have the law.'' So begins the author's sendup of a legal system in which suit piles on countersuit, deposition on decree. The novel won the 1994 National Book Award for fiction and was chosen by the editors of the Book Review as one of the best books of the year. Our reviewer, Robert Towers, said here last year that ``the novel's mockery of the legal profession, its minions, and its dupes, is extended to include a wide spectrum of contemporary American culture -- presented here, with corrosive Swiftian glee, as an unholy mess compounded of greed, ignorance, illiteracy, corruption and childish folly.''   HOW WE DIE: Reflections on Life's Final Chapter   By Sherwin B. Nuland. Vintage, $13.   The author, a surgeon and teacher, tells in plain language what actually happens to the body when a human being dies. This book, winner of the 1994 National Book Award for non-fiction, devotes chapters to each of the primary killers, including heart failure, cancer, and Alzheimer's disease. Our reviewer, William F. Buckley Jr., last year called Nuland ``a remarkable portraitist'' who ``quietly and informatively instructs and advises us.'' Vintage has also reissued Nuland's DOCTORS: The Biography of Medicine ($15), an account of healing from Hippocrates to organ transplants. Regina Morantz-Sanchez, writing here in 1988, said that rarely has ``the general reader been offered such a captivating narrative of biomedical matters.''   BRAZIL   By John Updike. Fawcett Crest, $6.99.   Unlikely lovers -- Tristao, a poor, black teen-ager from the slums of Rio de Janeiro, and Isabel, a rich, pampered, white girl -- flee to the wilds of western Brazil in the author's 16th novel. ``It is hard to resist the depth of his mind and the seduction of his prose,'' our reviewer, Barbara Kingsolver, said here last year.   IGNATIUS OF LOYOLA: The Psychology of a Saint   By W.W. Meissner. Yale University, $18.   The soldier who founded the Jesuit order is portrayed by a biographer who is a Jesuit priest and a psychoanalytically trained psychiatrist. This work is ``a touching, poignant, and straightforward biography, written with clarity and an erudition that rarely deteriorates into the pedantic,'' Robert Coles said here in 1992.   SAVING ST. GERM   By Carol Muske Dukes. Penguin, $10.95.   This novel looks inside the head of a biochemist lost in her work, who mentally builds the chemical structure of nicotine when she craves a cigarette, but is not cut out for the demands of academic and family life. In 1993, our reviewer, Tom De Haven, called this ``a truly original work of fiction,'' which explores ``a subculture that's baffling, often intimidating, to most of us.''   LOSING GROUND: American Social Policy, 1950-1980   By Charles Murray. Basic Books, $16.   As welfare reform moves to the front burner, the author -- who won some fame, or infamy, as co-author of ``The Bell Curve'' -- has added a new introduction to the 10th-anniversary edition of this book. It holds that many of the Great Society programs actually made the poor worse off. ``The argument is not new, but it is well made,'' Lawrence M. Mead said here in 1984.   THE GREEN KNIGHT   By Iris Murdoch. Penguin, $12.95.   The author evokes the story of Cain and Abel, and medieval romance, in the confrontation of two brothers in modern-day London. She ``interweaves allusions to a rich mine of sources: Freud, Sartre, Simone Weil, and Shakespeare,'' Linda Simon said here last year.   WITNESS AGAINST THE BEAST: William Blake and the Moral Law   By E.P. Thompson. New Press, $17.   In a study published just after his death in 1993, this eminent British historian reinterpreted Blake's poetry and found evidence of the religious outlook that shaped it. ``The union of knowledge with imagination and conviction is the mark of this book,'' Aileen Ward said here last year. ``It is a splendid conclusion to a life of great scholarship.'' In celebration of that life, The New Press has published Thompson's Making History: Writings on History and Culture ($17). It includes essays on such figures as William Morris and Mary Wollstonecraft.   PRONTO   By Elmore Leonard. Dell, $5.99.   For his 31st novel, the author has chosen a setting on the Italian Riviera, where a South Miami Beach bookie tries to escape his vengeful Mafia boss. The reader's payoff, our reviewer, Teresa Carpenter, said in 1993, ``is the glee with which Mr. Leonard sends up the mob.''   COMPLETE POEMS   By Marianne Moore. Penguin, $12.95.   Late in her life, Moore was anointed a cultural celebrity who posed for Life magazine at the zoo and won fame for her interest in baseball. This image, Hilton Kramer said here in 1981, ``was often grotesquely at odds with the very stringent and unyielding vision of her best writing.'' This collection, he wrote, is ``an important book, and one that every reader will want to have.''  </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950115.0016 type=story > <HEADLINE> MANSON JURORS SYMPATHIZE WITH SIMPSON PANELISTS </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> LOS ANGELES  (BC-SIMPSON-JURORS-3Takes-LADN) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_199501.gz"

"19684"	"he"	"wrote"	" The Spanish philosopher Jose Ortega y Gasset theorized about generations and destiny, speaking of a ``vital sensitivity'' shared by people who grew up together and experienced the same events, thoughts, sentiments, triumphs and disappointments. Each generation, he wrote, is ``a complete way of life that imprints itself indelibly on the individual.'' The achievement of the World War II generation was that it was able to imprint itself on much more than that, on the whole country. Their imprint has lasted more than a half century, and might even reach into the election that will choose the leader who will be president in the first years of the 21st century. Having led America into a new world, the GI Generation may also lead it into a new millennium. </P> "	"nyt_eng_199504.gz"

OKLA-BOMB-NICHOLS (El Reno, Okla.) _ An FBI affidavit suggests that planning for the bombing of the Oklahoma City Federal Building began as far back as September 1994, when Terry Nichols began buying thousands of pounds of ammonium nitrate fertilizer and renting several storage sheds in small towns in Kansas.  U.N.-NUKE-TREATY (United Nations) _ More than 170 nations agree to extend in perpetuity a treaty prohibiting the spread of nuclear arms. The Treaty on the Nonproliferation of Nuclear Weapons, a bedrock agreement in Soviet-American relations that survived the worst days of the cold war, became permanent by acclamation, without a vote. By Barbara Crossette.  With graphic.  CONGRESS-BUDGET (Washington) _ Following approval by the House Budget Committee of a seven-year fiscal plan that would change the face of the U.S. government, the Senate Budget Committee works to complete a companion measure. By David E. Rosenbaum.  With photo.  ENVIRON (Washington) _ The debate on pollution turns nasty with remarks from a California Republican about homosexuals and ``socialists.'' By John H. Cushman Jr.  ROCKEFELLER (New York) _ Rockefeller Center, a symbol of wealth, commerce and power the world over, is forced to file for bankruptcy protection by the Japanese company that controls it.  COURT-MARTIAL (New York) _ Career versus conscience: An Army officer who went off in search of violations of the rights of Haitian prisoners faces a court martial with his career and his conscience on the line. By Francis X. Clines.  COUNSELING-PREVENTION (New York) _ With New York City in the vanguard, the preventive approach to child welfare has spread to more than 35 states in the last 15 years, and now many preventive programs are likely to be dismantled because of budget cuts by New York City and New York state as well as proposed changes at the federal level. (HLW) By LAUREL GRAEBER c.1995 N.Y. Times News Service  ROALD DAHL: A Biography  By Jeremy Treglown. Harvest/Harcourt Brace, $13.  This study shows that the man who became famous for his books for children had not only the playful imagination associated with childhood, but also its propensity for tantrums and tactlessness. The biography ``does not at all suffer from being unauthorized,'' Ann Hulbert said here last year. She added: ``In fact, it is the perfect antidote to Dahl's own triumph at image making.''  HOUSE OF SPLENDID ISOLATION  By Edna O'Brien. Plume, $10.95.  Josie O'Meara is alone in her bitterness and widowhood when fate and circumstance bring a visitor to her door: McGreevy, an Irish Republican Army terrorist who occupies her home and takes her hostage. Both have been betrayed _ he by one of his confederates, she by a former servant who still lingers at the edges of her life. In 1994 our reviewer, John L'Heureux, called their story ``a fascinating and disturbing novel.'' As the characters struggle, he wrote, ``we feel that for the moment we are looking deep into the conflicted mind and heart of contemporary Ireland.'' The misery of the country is also reflected in MAY THE LORD IN HIS MERCY BE KIND TO BELFAST, by Tony Parker (Owl/Holt, $13.95), an account in the voices of real men and women, some Roman Catholic, some Protestant, some soldiers, some civilians. The author is ``an extraordinary reporter,'' Margo Jefferson said in The New York Times last year. His work, she noted, ``is exceptional.''  FROM THE TEETH OF ANGELS  By Jonathan Carroll. Main Street/Doubleday, $12.  Death is a character here, and he's quite an unsavory fellow. He enjoys playing games with his prey, which includes a former television host with cancer and an HIV-positive photojournalist. The result is ``a fine and harrowing and beautiful work of fiction,'' Tom De Haven wrote in the Book Review in 1994.  THE BEAK OF THE FINCH: A Story of Evolution in Our Time  By Jonathan Weiner. Vintage, $13.  This Pulitzer Prize-winning study allows the reader to visualize what the author actually saw when he reported on the work of Peter and Rosemary Grant. They are biologists who have documented that the thickness of the beaks of certain finches varies according to the type of seeds the climate makes available. Weiner's ``triumph is to reveal how evolution and science as a whole work,'' Douglas H. Chadwick said in these pages last year. People, too, are subject to natural selection, explains THE RED QUEEN: Sex and the Evolution of Human Nature, by Matt Ridley (Penguin, $12.95). But mating behavior, not weather, seems to direct our species' survival. Ridley ``makes a convincing case that sex itself originated in an arms race between parasites and their hosts,'' Derek Bickerton wrote here in 1994.  SINGING SONGS  By Meg Tilly. Plume, $9.95.  This actress's first fiction is narrated by a young girl, Anna, who begins the story in 1965, when she is 4{. The subject is childhood, which in this novel is conquered and defiled by a cruel and abusive stepfather. Last year, our reviewer, Donna Rifkind, praised ``the remarkable coherence and clarity of Anna's voice.''  CERTAIN TRUMPETS: The Call of Leaders  By Garry Wills. Touchstone/Simon &AMP; Schuster, $14.  The author explores the quality of leadership, defining as successful those who share a common goal with their followers. He presents profiles of 16 such people, ranging from King David to Martha Graham, along with personal sketches of their opposing types, like Solomon and Madonna. The book offers ``plenty of lively narrative and argument,'' Frank Kermode said here in 1994.  JULIP  By Jim Harrison. Washington Square/Pocket Books, $12.  The masculine myths celebrated by Hemingway are dissected _ and deflated _ in these three novellas. The men are no match for women like the title character, a latter-day Artemis with her own hunting dogs. Writing in these pages last year, Jonis Agee said the author ``has established himself as a genuinely comic writer.''  REMEMBERING SATAN  By Lawrence Wright. Vintage, $12.  This book examines the case of Paul R. Ingram, a former deputy sheriff who is in prison for raping his daughters even though there was no evidence of the crimes and a psychologist hired by the prosecution concluded that the charges _ and Ingram's confession _ were the products of highly susceptible imaginations. Last year, our reviewer, Walter Reich, found the book ``stunning.'' It is the author's ``cautionary tale about the dangers of so-called recovered memories,'' he said. Vintage has also reissued Wright's SAINTS AND SINNERS ($12), portraits of six men and women, from Jimmy Swaggart to Madalyn Murray O'Hair, for whom religion is the defining issue of their lives. In 1993, one critic called it ``a serenely passionate chronicle of the cobbled byways of the human spirit.''  MALLORY'S ORACLE  By Carol O'Connell. Jove, $5.99.  Sgt. Kathleen Mallory is a former child thief who has become the computer whiz of the NYPD. She draws on all her talents to track down a serial killer whose victims include her adoptive father. This is ``a powerful novel'' whose readers will be ``well rewarded,'' Andrew Vachss wrote in the Book Review in 1994.  THE SIXTEEN PLEASURES  By Robert Hellenga. Delta, $11.95.  The title refers to the erotica in a 16th-century volume entrusted to the narrator, a book conservator in the flooded Florence of 1966. This is ``a lively first novel that communicates the heady peril'' of the time, Mark Mitchell said here last year. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950618.0031 type=story > <HEADLINE> BINDING UP OLD WOUNDS </HEADLINE> <DATELINE>  (BC-VIETNAM-NEWSWEEK-NYTSF) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_199506.gz"

Times News Service  (NOTE: Because of the complicated figuring involved, you are requested to use these Best Sellers lists in full or not at all. This particularly applies to the Paperbacks. The ``Advice, How-To and Miscellaneous'' listings were separated from the non-fiction list for important reasons, and not to print ``Advice, How-To and Miscellaneous'' gives an impression that is incorrect, resulting in criticism from publishing houses.)  Rankings reflect sales figures for the week ending June 11, 1995.  The listings are based on computer-processed sales figures from 3,050 bookstores and from representative wholesalers with more than 38,000 retail outlets, including newsstands, variety stores, supermarkets, and bookstores. These figures are statistically adjusted to represent sales in all such outlets across the United States. The number of titles within the two subdivisions of non-fiction can change from week to week, reflecting changes in book buying.  An `x' following a book's ranking indicates that its sales, weighted to reflect the book-selling industry nationally, are barely distinguishable from those of the book above. "25608"	"he"	"wrote"	" Justice Nolan dissented. In his view, the Council ``does not need a narrow or distinct theme or message in its parade for it to be protected under the First Amendment.'' Id., at 256, 636 N. E. 2d, at 1303. First, he wrote, even if the parade had no message at all, GLIB's particular message could not be forced upon it. Id., at 257, 636 N. E. 2d, at 1303, citing Wooley v. Maynard, 430 U. S. 705, 717 (1977) (state requirement to display ``Live Free or Die'' on license plates violates First Amendment). Second, according to Justice Nolan, the trial judge clearly erred in finding the parade devoid of expressive purpose. Ibid. He would have held that the Council, like any expressive association, cannot be barred from excluding applicants who do not share the views the Council wishes to advance. Id., at 257-259, 636 N. E. 2d, at 1303-1304, citing Roberts v. United States Jaycees, 468 U. S. 609 (1984). Under either a pure speech or associational theory, the State's purpose of eliminating discrimination on the basis of sexual orientation, according to the dissent, could be achieved by more narrowly drawn means, such as ordering admission of individuals regardless of sexual preference, without taking the further step of prohibiting the Council from editing the views expressed in their parade. Id., at 256, 258, 636 N. E. 2d, at 1302, 1304. In Justice Nolan's opinion, because GLIB's message was separable from the status of its members, such a narrower order would accommodate the State's interest without the likelihood of infringing on the Council's First Amendment rights. Finally, he found clear error in the trial judge's equation of exclusion on the basis of GLIB's message with exclusion on the basis of its members' sexual orientation. To the dissent this appeared false in the light of ``overwhelming evidence'' that the Council objected to GLIB on account of its message and a dearth of testimony or documentation indicating that sexual orientation was the bar to admission. Id., at 260, 636 N. E. 2d, at 1304. The dissent accordingly concluded that the Council had not even violated the State's public accommodations law.We granted certiorari to determine whether the requirement to admit a parade contingent expressing a message not of the private organizers' own choosing violates the First Amendment. 513 U. S. --- (1995). We hold that it does and reverse. </P> "	"nyt_eng_199506.gz"

To pass along one of them, he has offered to wash lawyers' feet.  Litchfield's offer, presented in a letter to every lawyer in Nevada County, arises from his view of the relationship between a judge and those who appear before him. While attending a ``Living Last Supper'' presentation at the First Baptist Church on Easter, he wrote, he realized how to make a ``gesture of my desire to serve you. ... I offer to wash the feet of every single attorney in Nevada County. This offer specifically includes the officers of the Nevada County Bar Association. In fact, I will come to the next County Bar Association meeting, if I am asked, and will perform the foot-washing service.'' His letter specifies that symbolic foot-washing existed ``before it became a religious gesture, and you do not have to be a Christian to participate in it.''  In a phone conversation, Litchfield described lawyers as victims of a kind of generalized hatred. ``We all know about the crude hate words, the n-word, the k-word. How many times in your life have you heard people say the word `lawyer' with the same tone as they say hate words? Why is that acceptable in this culture? I've actually said it myself, and I am one of them.  ``Here's a new class of people,'' said Litchfield, ``that are so dehumanized, it's time to start ministering to them.''  PICASSO'S LOVER ON HIS PORTRAIT OF HER  ``Picasso and Portraiture: Representation and Transformation,'' a new exhibit of the master's work, opens at New York's Museum of Modern Art on Sunday. A number of subjects discuss their own responses to Picasso's portraits of them in Sunday's New York Times magazine.  Art critic Michael Kimmelman notes that Picasso's portraits of his longtime mistress, Francoise Gilot, haven't received the praise earned by portraits of other subjects. ``Because the ones of me are more elusive,'' says Gilot.  ``There's more of this sort of secret language in them. Also because I did not altogether expose my inner self to him. ... My portraits are not boxes in which I fit. I'm not a prisoner of them.''  Gilot is the only woman who ever left Picasso, writes Kimmelman. ``Painting women's portraits was one way Picasso thought he seduced them,'' says Gilot. ``I told him, `I love you and it has nothing to do with that. I love you as you and, as a painter, but whether you do or do not paint me doesn't matter.' ... I felt entirely free and independent of his portraits. I did not define myself by them, put myself inside them.''  LIFE AND DEATH  _ Twenty-three percent of dog owners carry pictures of their pets in their wallets, says Health magazine.  _ Californians who live in the 916 and 707 area codes can dial (900) 225-BUGS, and pay $2 for the first minute, $1 for each minute thereafter, to have their insects and spiders identified.  This new program at UC Davis makes entomology students and staff members available between 10 a.m. and 4 p.m. If the phone panel is stumped, they may request that a sample be mailed in. Complicated requests will be referred to a non-toll phone line.  ``The process is a lot like medical diagnostics,'' says entomologist Lynn Kimsey, ``except we'll be diagnosing an organism rather than a disease.''  This service isn't available yet to residents in the 415, 510 and 408 area codes, but organizers hope to expand it according to demand.  _ Dade County, Fla., Metro Commissioner Natacha Millan has instituted special parking permits for parents with children under the age of two. Parking lots are required to mark designated spaces for permit-holders.  ``It's a safety issue as much as a convenience issue,'' Millan told Family Circle. ``Parents lugging kids and strollers are vulnerable.'' Violators will be fined up to $250. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19960425.0675 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-LEAH-PERSONALS-SFCHRON) </DATELINE> <TEXT> (Eds: This story also moved in the ``l'' lifestyle file) By LEAH GARCHIK c.1996 San Francisco Chronicle  A CANDIDATE MINISTERS TO LAWYERS  Robert Litchfield Jr. of Roseville, Calif., who has been practicing law for 16 years and is a religious man, says in a self-effacing way that surviving a March primary to run for Superior Court judge in Nevada County was a kind of miracle. ``God put me here in this place to send out some very specific messages,'' he says. Anthony from the Capitol Crypt to the Rotunda, Arlys Angelique Endres, 9, of Phoenix launched her own nationwide campaign.  Since February, the third-grader has spent an hour and a half every night writing letters asking for money, says the Washington newspaper Roll Call. Her mother spends an hour every day tracking down addresses. A thousand letters have raised a total of $500.  Arlys had hoped that politicians, friends and private women's groups would contribute, and she hoped to collect the money in Susan B. Anthonydollar coins only. Response was so small, however, that she changed her plan. Nowadays, she is collecting dollar bills marked with the initials ``SBA'' for her heroine.  Her mother emphasized to Roll Call that her daughter's campaign isn't based in radicalism and that the household is ``conservative. ... This is a traditional family. My husband and I have been together for 16 years and we're boring as heck.''  Arlys, a cheerleader, sent pleas for contributions to every professional cheerleading group in the country, along with pictures of herself with her pompons. The Dallas Cowboys Cheerleaders,  the only respondents, sent a single dollar.  SBA bucks, in singles or in multiples, may be sent to Arlys Endres, 3936 West Solar Drive, Phoenix, AZ 85051.  WHO SAID WHAT  ``Margaux's very spiritual, and she's into all that metaphysical stuff. She does yoga, she goes to the sweat lodge, she works with the Indians. Margaux does not do things for money.''  _ Graham Kaye, agent for Margaux Hemingway, after Hemingway signed on to let a psychic phone hotline use her name. Kaye was quoted in New York magazine.  ``A cannoli cream has a shelf life of two to three weeks if it's refrigerated. A Danish, on the other hand, is dead in a day.''  _ Deconstruction of ``Hamlet,'' from Pizza and Pasta magazine.  BORN TOO SOON  Keeping in mind the showy comeback of Julie Andrews in Broadway's ``Victor/Victoria,'' Inside Media's Ed Martin wondered why the star's face hasn't graced the covers of any of the women's magazines.  Andrews' tale, he wrote, is ``just the kind of celebrity rise and fall and rise story the women's service field loves to tell. So we asked around, only to be told that Andrews was thought to be a little long in the tooth.''  ``The Sisters,'' he writes, referring to the bevy of magazines that combine celebrity profiles with beauty, health, gardening and marriage stories, ``like their stars younger.''  THE PLEASURES OF SUMMER  ``The one and only Cherry Blossom Festival, Richmond, Va., in the '60s: 10 bands in a stadium, 25,000 people on the field. About halfway into it, narcs start busting people. Next, a police helicopter flies over the crowd. Three school buses unload riot police. A kid starts a bulldozer from a site next door, crushes seven police cars. As we get on, the police enter the stadium. People are throwing bottles _ it's a full-tilt riot. So I dedicate `The Star-Spangled Banner' to the police. They take their helmets off, and the kids stop, too. You know, it's always good to have an experienced hand at the tiller.''  _ Steve Miller recalling summer raves, in Rolling Stone.  WOULD YOU LIKE TO SWING ON A BAR  After surveying families returning from vacation, scientists at Club Med have determined that ``89 percent of children who overcame their fear of participating in an activity like the trapeze demonstrated an increased willingness to go to bed alone after they returned home.''  WORSE THAN A BARK  Before petting a strange dog, let it sniff the back of your hand, don't present your palm or fingers, don't crowd it, don't squeal loudly, don't engage in rough play, don't swing your toys around and don't turn and flee.  This advice for dealing with man's best friend _ it works, too, for dealing with human best friends _ comes from the Oakland (Calif.) WASHINGTON  SQUADRON _ WASHINGTON _ Evidence of ``lewd behavior and debauchery'' among a squadron of Air Force pilots has prompted a top-ranking general to warn that such conduct will not be tolerated _ but not to acknowledge his own alleged membership in a secret drinking organization implicated in the reported misconduct. In a memorandum signed a week ago, Gen. John G. Lorber, commander of all Pacific Air Force bases, ordered officers under his command to hold meetings to discuss codes of conduct and ethics in the Air Force. His concern was aroused, he wrote, by a ``recent event'' involving members of a squadron who ``conducted themselves as though they were in a wild fraternity, totally out of control with no mature supervision.'' One of the details not listed in the general's memorandum was the involvement of members of a secret association of several hundred Air Force officers known as ``the Command Barstool Association'' in the alleged misconduct. According to sworn testimony in a recent Alaska court-martial, Gen. Lorber is ``a stooler.'' (Nesmith and Carollo).  MEDICARE _ WASHINGTON _ If President Clinton is re-elected, his administration will seek a `quick fix'' compromise with Congress to shore up the Medicare trust fund while a bipartisan commission works out longer-term solutions, Health and Human Services Secretary Donna Shalala said Wednesday. She said the ``quick fix'' could add about 10 years to the life of the federal health care program for the elderly, which is currently facing bankruptcy by 2001. It would involve cuts in payments to doctors and hospitals while avoiding increases in taxes or patients' costs, she said. (Lipman).  IMMIGRATION _ WASHINGTON _ The Senate immigration subcommittee holds hearing on naturalization procedures amid charges that the Immigration and Naturalization Service skimped on background checks in order to swear in more new U.S. citizens, who tend to vote Democratic. (Harlan).  CHURCH-ARSON _ WASHINGTON _ The U.S. Commission on Civil Rights holds a news conference to discuss information its new report showing that pervasive racism and segregation in the South have contributed to the recent church burnings in that region. (Moscoso).  POLITICS  DEBATE _ ST. PETERSBURG, Fla. _ In a clash of both style and substance, Vice President Gore defends the record of the last four years against the jabs of tax-cutting enthusiast Jack Kemp. The 90-minute debate could answer the question about whether Gore can shed his image of stiffness, and whether Kemp the ``happy warrior'' can land some punches. (Malone).  With DEBATE-HIGHLIGHTS _ ST. PETERSBURG, Fla. _ The high points issue-by-issue from the vice presidential debate. (Moscoso).  EDs: Stories will move by 11:30 p.m. EDT with and update by 11:50. (th) By LAWRIE MIFFLIN c.1996 N.Y. Times News Service  He is an obsessive Knicks fan and has to work late. He has no access to a television or a radio, not even a telephone to call Sportsphone. Solution: a new gadget called Sports Trax, about the size of a personal pager, on which he can punch a basketball-shaped button and get the latest score of the Knicks' game, minute by minute, basket by basket.  When this fanatic looks at the display screen on his $200 toy, is he simply checking out a news report, or is he watching a virtual performance, misappropriated from the National Basketball Association?  Monday, Sports Team Analysis and Tracking Systems Inc. (known as Stats Inc.), which gathers the data, and Motorola Inc., which makes the pager, are appealing an injunction against the dissemination of the NBA scores that was issued by Judge Loretta A. Preska in arguments before a three-judge panel of the 2nd U.S. Circuit Court of Appeals in Manhattan.  Because of the First Amendment implications of the case, several news organizations, including The New York Times, have filed friend-of-the-court briefs supporting the defendants, contending that their devices merely transmit ``bare-bones facts'' of the game, with no sense of the plays being made, who scored a basket or how, or any of the game's drama except the changing score.  But NBC, another news organization, filed an amicus brief for the plaintiffs, the NBA and NBA Properties Inc., contending that a line must be drawn to prevent new media like on-line services from taking away the value of a live game or television broadcast of the game. NBC has a four-year, $750 million contract to broadcast NBA games through the 1997-98 season.  ``I was surprised and I was troubled that a major news organization like NBC would take a position that in my judgment is contrary to the First Amendment,'' said Robin Bierstedt, vice president and deputy general counsel of Time Inc., which has no role in this case. ``A basketball game is news, not a performance, and as such should not subject to a proprietary interest of a party.''  Richard Cotton, executive vice president and general counsel at NBC, a unit of General Electric Co., argued that Stats Inc.'s transmissions of the score, the name of the team with possession of the ball and the time remaining in the game could not be construed as news reporting deserving of First Amendment protection.  ``No news organization is in the business of continuous, real-time transmitting of information,'' he said. He contended that, whether televised, broadcast on radio or sent out as continuous updating of numerical scores, such transmissions were not news but virtual recreations of games.  ``It's a real-time presentation of what's going on, on a continuous, nonstop basis,'' he said. ``That's the essence of radio rights, of television rights, and if someone wants to do exactly that on line, he should also have to pay for those rights.''  Stats operates its up-to-the-minute data service by having people listen to radios or watch televisions in various cities and continuously enter the game scores on computers, which transmit the scores to Stats' on-line service and to a satellite service, which beams the information to anyone punching a Sports Trax pager.  The company said that it was merely relaying factual information already in the public domain, not unlike what a fan could get on all-news radio, or the Cable News Network, or Sportsphone.  Sportsphone updates scores every few minutes to callers. But Sportsphone has agreed to abide by limits the NBA has set on how frequently press representatives may issue updates (three times a quarter, with expanded information allowed at halftime and after the game), in exchange for press credentials to attend games.  ``The NBA has taken a position, basically, that unless you are one of their accredited media, or you subscribe to data from their accredited media, you have no right to the information,'' said Andrew L. Deutsch, a partner in Piper &AMP; Marbury, the law firm representing Stats. ``My client's position is it has a right to use any information that's in the public domain.''  Though Judge Preska denied the NBA's claim of copyright infringement (because copyright law protects creative expression in a copyrighted work, but not facts included in the work), she upheld the league's misappropriation claim.  ``I find without hesitation,'' she wrote in her Sept. 3 ruling, ``that these products cross the boundary from mere media coverage of NBA games into competing commercial appropriation of these games.''  She pointed out that the NBA has its own statistics-gathering service, Gamestats, and has plans for selling its data to others. That, she wrote, means Stats' America Online site and the Sports Trax pager are competitors who ``undoubtedly dilute the exclusivity, and thus the value, of the rights the NBA has to offer.''  But Deutsch and his colleagues argue that federal copyright law supersedes New York state misappropriation law. ``You can't use state law to make private property what federal law has declared belongs to the public,'' he said.  He and those who filed briefs supporting Stats' case challenged the judge's interpretation of what is commercially attractive about an NBA game, saying that it is the drama, not the data.  ``A running score is not an account of the game,'' Deutsch said. ``An account is the detailed call of a game, the effort by radio and TV announcers to give you the best seat in the stands, to give you an experience as close as possible of attending the sporting event. People don't go into the arena to raise their heads and look at the scoreboard for 48 minutes.''  George Freeman, assistant general counsel for The New York Times Co., said the NBA demeaned its own product by arguing that numbers displayed on a pager or a computer screen are ``anything like seeing slam dunks or behind-the-back passes.''  ``The notion of someone saying, `Honey, I've got to run into the den and see how the game's going on my computer' rather than on TV is ridiculous,'' Freeman said.  In his amicus brief, Freeman argued that the NBA acknowledged that scores and statistics themselves were not NBA property, in its warning before every telecast, which begins ``This program is authorized under rights granted by the National Basketball Association,'' and forbids unauthorized use of ``the pictures, descriptions or accounts'' of the game, not the numerical facts.  Still, Cotton, NBC's general counsel, said he hoped this case would help strike a new balance between commercial rights and news-reporting rights in the era of new media like on-line services.  ``The technology's only going to get better, so you'll wind up being able to add who scored, how they scored and so on,'' he said. And when that happens in real time, he said, he sees a competitive problems _ when news can be transmitted so fast that it is no longer a news report, but becomes virtual reality. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19961020.0240 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-PAGER-LEGAL-MEDIA-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> COMPANY THAT PROVIDES SCORES VIA PAGER TO APPEAL RULING (DUPLICATION WARNING: Also moving in ``f'' news file.) Because of the First Amendment implications of the case, several news organizations, including The New York Times, have filed friend-of-the-court briefs supporting the defendants, contending that their devices merely transmit ``bare-bones facts'' of the game, with no sense of the plays being made, who scored a basket or how, or any of the game's drama except the changing score.  But NBC, another news organization, filed an amicus brief for the plaintiffs, the NBA and NBA Properties Inc., contending that a line must be drawn to prevent new media like on-line services from taking away the value of a live game or television broadcast of the game. NBC has a four-year, $750 million contract to broadcast NBA games through the 1997-98 season.  ``I was surprised and I was troubled that a major news organization like NBC would take a position that in my judgment is contrary to the First Amendment,'' said Robin Bierstedt, vice president and deputy general counsel of Time Inc., which has no role in this case. ``A basketball game is news, not a performance, and as such should not subject to a proprietary interest of a party.''  Richard Cotton, executive vice president and general counsel at NBC, a unit of General Electric Co., argued that Stats Inc.'s transmissions of the score, the name of the team with possession of the ball and the time remaining in the game could not be construed as news reporting deserving of First Amendment protection.  ``No news organization is in the business of continuous, real-time transmitting of information,'' he said. He contended that, whether televised, broadcast on radio or sent out as continuous updating of numerical scores, such transmissions were not news but virtual recreations of games.  ``It's a real-time presentation of what's going on, on a continuous, nonstop basis,'' he said. ``That's the essence of radio rights, of television rights, and if someone wants to do exactly that on line, he should also have to pay for those rights.''  Stats operates its up-to-the-minute data service by having people listen to radios or watch televisions in various cities and continuously enter the game scores on computers, which transmit the scores to Stats' on-line service and to a satellite service, which beams the information to anyone punching a Sports Trax pager.  The company said that it was merely relaying factual information already in the public domain, not unlike what a fan could get on all-news radio, or the Cable News Network, or Sportsphone.  Sportsphone updates scores every few minutes to callers. But Sportsphone has agreed to abide by limits the NBA has set on how frequently press representatives may issue updates (three times a quarter, with expanded information allowed at halftime and after the game), in exchange for press credentials to attend games.  ``The NBA has taken a position, basically, that unless you are one of their accredited media, or you subscribe to data from their accredited media, you have no right to the information,'' said Andrew L. Deutsch, a partner in Piper &AMP; Marbury, the law firm representing Stats. ``My client's position is it has a right to use any information that's in the public domain.''  Though Judge Preska denied the NBA's claim of copyright infringement (because copyright law protects creative expression in a copyrighted work, but not facts included in the work), she upheld the league's misappropriation claim.  ``I find without hesitation,'' she wrote in her Sept. 3 ruling, ``that these products cross the boundary from mere media coverage of NBA games into competing commercial appropriation of these games.''  She pointed out that the NBA has its own statistics-gathering service, Gamestats, and has plans for selling its data to others. That, she wrote, means Stats' America Online site and the Sports Trax pager are competitors who ``undoubtedly dilute the exclusivity, and thus the value, of the rights the NBA has to offer.''  But Deutsch and his colleagues argue that federal copyright law supersedes New York state misappropriation law. ``You can't use state law to make private property what federal law has declared belongs to the public,'' he said.  He and those who filed briefs supporting Stats' case challenged the judge's interpretation of what is commercially attractive about an NBA game, saying that it is the drama, not the data.  ``A running score is not an account of the game,'' Deutsch said. ``An account is the detailed call of a game, the effort by radio and TV announcers to give you the best seat in the stands, to give you an experience as close as possible of attending the sporting event. People don't go into the arena to raise their heads and look at the scoreboard for 48 minutes.''  George Freeman, assistant general counsel for The New York Times Co., said the NBA demeaned its own product by arguing that numbers displayed on a pager or a computer screen are ``anything like seeing slam dunks or behind-the-back passes.''  ``The notion of someone saying, `Honey, I've got to run into the den and see how the game's going on my computer' rather than on TV is ridiculous,'' Freeman said.  In his amicus brief, Freeman argued that the NBA acknowledged that scores and statistics themselves were not NBA property, in its warning before every telecast, which begins ``This program is authorized under rights granted by the National Basketball Association,'' and forbids unauthorized use of ``the pictures, descriptions or accounts'' of the game, not the numerical facts.  Still, Cotton, NBC's general counsel, said he hoped this case would help strike a new balance between commercial rights and news-reporting rights in the era of new media like on-line services.  ``The technology's only going to get better, so you'll wind up being able to add who scored, how they scored and so on,'' he said. And when that happens in real time, he said, he sees a competitive problems _ when news can be transmitted so fast that it is no longer a news report, but becomes virtual reality. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19961020.0241 type=story > <HEADLINE> WEIGHTING COSTS OF INTERNET ACCESS FOR EVERY SCHOOL, LIBRARY </HEADLINE> <DATELINE>  (BC-INTERNET-ACCESS-642&AMP;ADD-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_199610.gz"

The deal, which cleared up questions about services and agriculture, is is expected to be sealed at a trade meeting in November.  EUROPE  LITHUANIA: EX-COMMUNIST NAMED PREMIER  Lithuania's last Communist boss and popular former president, Algirdas Brazauskas, won parliament's approval as the next prime minister after a center-right coalition collapsed in disputes over privatization and the pace of economic reforms. Brazauskas, who has two weeks to form a government, is expected to place greater emphasis on social welfare programs for people in the former Soviet republic but President Valdas Adamkus warned that he would not tolerate any government that undermined Lithuania's strategic goals for reform and entry into the European Union.  GREECE: FIRST MAD COW CASE  The government announced Greece's first case of mad cow disease. The infection in a dairy cow in the north of the country was detected in routine testing dictated by European Union regulations in response to the recent oubreak of the disease in Europe. Cases have been confirmed in 13 countries in Western Europe and in the Czech Republic but the vast majority have been in Britain.  ITALY: BERLUSCONI MEETS WITH POPE  Prime Minister Silvio Berlusconi had his first private audience with John Paul II since taking office last month. The visit gave the leaders a chance to discuss the pope's pet projects ranging from state support for Catholic schools to globalization and Third World debt relief. As they parted, the 81-year-old pope said to Berlusconi, 64, ``You are a youngster, I am not.''  ASIA  CHINA: A JOB FOR ALL THE KING'S MEN  The final pieces of a Navy surveillance plane whose collision with a Chinese fighter on April 1 scarred relations between Washington and Beijing were flown from China's territory in a giant Russian-designed cargo plane. The parts were headed for Marietta, Ga., where engineers will determine whether the EP-3E Aries can be put back together and flown again, military officials said.  INDONESIA: SEPARATIST VIOLENCE CONTINUES  Two days after the end of peace talks between the government and separatist forces in the province of Aceh, officials said at least six people had been killed in renewed violence. On Sunday, relief workers said they had exhumed the bodies of 27 people who had apparently been recently killed by government forces.  SOUTH KOREA: TRAFFIC IN NORTHERNERS  Desperate North Koreans fleeing their impoverished communist country are getting into the South on Chinese boats with the help of local brokers, South Korean officials said. The police arrested five suspected brokers and six Koreans they said had entered illegally.  UNITED NATIONS  JAPAN PLEDGES $200 MILLION FOR DISEASE FUND  Japan will give an initial contribution of $200 million to a global fund to fight AIDS, malaria and tuberculosis, matching the pledge from the Bush administration. Prime Minister Junichiro Koizumi was formerly a health minister and has supported spending on international health programs. Japan leads the industrial nations in the amount of foreign aid to developing countries.  AFRICA  CONGO: LEADERS TO MEET  President Joseph Kabila and President Yoweri Museveni of Uganda are scheduled to meet in Tanzania on Wednesday in what would be the first such meeting since Kabila succeeded his assassinated father in January. Uganda, with Rwanda, has been backing rebels opposed to Congo's government of Kinshasa for the last three years. Tanzania's foreign ministry said the talk would focus on bilateral relations.  NIGERIA: FIGHTING UPROOTS 72,000  More than 72,000 people were displaced in two states after the outbreak of two separate ethnic clashes in the last month, according to the Nigerian Red Cross. Muslim Hausas and Christian Jarawas fought over the imposition of Islamic law in one northern state, causing 22,000 people to flee; another 50,000 are said to have fled another state after clashes between the Azara and Tiv ethnic groups over the kiling of a traditional ruler.  ZIMBABWE: UNIONS CALL TWO-DAY STRIKE  The Zimbabwe Congress of Trade Unions called on workers to stay home in a two-day strike that left many businesses shuttered. The workers were protesting a recent increase in gasoline prices. The government declared the strike illegal and sent riot police on patrol, but observers reported that the streets were peaceful and the police reported only a handful of arrests. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20010703.0243 type=story > <HEADLINE> PILOTS PROTEST AMERICAN'S PLAN TO FLY AT A LOWER ALTITUDE </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> WASHINGTON (BC-AMR-PILOT-PROTEST-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_200107.gz"

G. Sebald. (New Directions, $13.95.) A rich stew of history, fiction, reporting, fantasy, travel notes, autobiography, photographs and illustrations, this German-born author's first novel is a spiraling inquiry into the workings of memory, as the narrator reflects on episodes in European history and how they relate to his own origins and inner life. The result is an ``extraordinary, unclassifiable book'' by ``a thrilling, original writer,'' W. S. Di Piero wrote in The New York Times Book Review last year. ``He makes narration a state of investigative bliss.''  GOD'S NAME IN VAIN: The Wrongs and Rights of Religion in Politics, by Stephen L. Carter. (Basic Books, $15.) A Yale law professor and evangelical Episcopalian argues that a religious voice should be welcome in American politics, but warns that religion can lose its integrity and moral bearings when it strays too far into civic affairs. Last year the reviewer Brent Staples found this a provocative, if flawed, book. For one thing, he wrote, ``Carter's contention ... that the state should cede more power to religion will strike many people as a recipe for warfare in the streets.''  PROPERTIES OF LIGHT, by Rebecca Goldstein. (Mariner/Houghton Mifflin, $13.) In a Gothic novel that explores the darker side of the quest for scientific truth, two physicists who share a noble dream of reconciling the principles of quantum mechanics and relativity become locked instead in a collaboration that leads to hatred, treachery and violence. Last year, Sylvia Brownrigg admired the book's ``cool grace'' and its ``luminous, incendiary imagery.''  AN OBEDIENT FATHER, by Akhil Sharma. (Harvest/Harcourt, $13.) Ram Karan, the main narrator of this ambitious first novel by a young New York investment banker, is a corrupt functionary in the Delhi school system, a vile, guilt-ridden schemer who somehow elicits more pity than disgust despite his voracious appetites. ``At its best'' the tale ``displays some of the most psychologically acute writing to have come out of modern India,'' Akash Kapur wrote in The New York Times Book Review in 2000. ``Sharma's evenhanded treatment of men like Karan ... is wise and compassionate.''  HOME ICE: Reflections on Backyard Rinks and Frozen Ponds, by Jack Falla. (McGregor, $14.95.) In a paean to small-time hockey and its enthusiasts, a sportswriter tells of his lifelong infatuation with the sport and how his homemade rink at his New England home became a gathering ground for family and friends. The result is ``literary hot chocolate that will warm your heart,'' Robert Lipsyte wrote in The New York Times last year.  HILDA AND PEARL, by Alice Mattison. (Perennial/HarperCollins, $13.) An 11-year-old girl coming of age in New York during the McCarthy era uncovers the long-held secrets of her mother and aunt, whose passionate bond endures in the face of tragic events that include a painful betrayal and a baby's death. Mattison renders her characters' working-class lives ``in spare, graceful detail,'' Joyce Reiser Kornblatt said in 1995. ``Forgiving and wise, `Hilda and Pearl' is a memorable novel about love's resilience.''  EASTWARD TO TARTARY: Travels in the Balkans, the Middle East, and the Caucasus, by Robert D. Kaplan. (Vintage Departures, $14.) A prolific travel writer and political journalist shuttles readers across an immense region stretching from Bulgaria to Baku and beyond, recounting his own journeys and examining with hard-nosed realism the ``new Silk Roads'' winding through newly opened lands. ``Erudite and intrepid,'' with a wealth ``of elite contacts all over the world, he is a deft guide,'' Laura Secor wrote last year.  The same publisher has reissued SOLDIERS OF GOD: With Islamic Warriors in Afghanistan and Pakistan ($14), Kaplan's 1990 account of living among the mujahedeen fighting the Soviets in the 1980's and of the rise of the Taliban. The book, which has been updated with a new introduction and final chapter, is ``affecting and informative,'' one critic said.  JAYBER CROW, by Wendell Berry. (Counterpoint, $15.) Set in a fading Kentucky town, this rough-hewn novel by a renowned poet tells of a young man who abandons his plans to become a minister and becomes the village barber instead. By the end of his elegiac life story, ``this melancholy barber has won both our attention and our hearts,'' Gail Gilliland said in 2000. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20011110.0209 type=story > <HEADLINE> LEAF EAGER FOR A FRESH START WITH COWBOYS </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> ATLANTA (BC-FBN-LEAF-COWBOYS-TEX) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_200111.gz"

Luckily, this is an illustrated biography, and a handsome one, although not as voluminous as the authors' previous books (''The Civil War,'' ``Baseball,'' ``Jazz''). Twain was born in a shack in Missouri. After he saw a picture of it, he wrote, ``I have always stated it was a palace, but I shall be more guarded now.'' He grew up to live in a great riot of a house in Connecticut, where his daughters adored him despite his volcanic temper and he loved and honored his wife, Olivia (when he first saw her, Twain, who was as adept with a compliment as he was at imagining scrappy Missouri boys, told a friend that she was ``fearfully and wonderfully made''). There are a million great anecdotes about him, but if you've read them, the illustrations still make this book worth its price. There are wonderful family photographs, pages from his manuscripts, pictures of colleagues and friends, and, of course, of Twain himself. He was dapper even in his coffin, as a blur of admirers shuffled by. </P> </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20011216.0110 type=story > <HEADLINE> Standards don't apply to those president OKs </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> ATHENS, Ga. They streak through the Salone Internazional del Mobile in Milan, (that's the Milan Furniture Fair to you and me), and either fade as quickly as a tea rose or find favor. A very few will eventually become enduring icons of their age. The trip from Milan to icon is perilous. On the dress page:  ENRON-CLASS-POLITICS-REVIEW (Washington) _ Once they express their requisite sympathy for stockholders and investors in Enron, many Democrats are downright giddy over the company's implosion. Not since Watergate and Richard M. Nixon's cozying up to corporate bigwigs wielding bags of money, they say, has their party had such an ideal vehicle to arouse the citizenry and skewer a Republican president as favoring monied interests. But some Democrats caution their party about rushing headlong into making Enron a political issue. By Richard L. Berke. WITH PHOTO. (Moved in the `a' category)  BUSH-CONGRESS-REVIEW (Undated) _ Wartime concentrates power in the presidency. But abuses of capitalism _ especially when they are Enron-sized and presided over by a president's friends _ tend to swing the pendulum the other way, as Congress acts to investigate, condemn and re-regulate. So what happens when war and scandal, a powerful president and an ascendant Congress all take the stage at the same time? The president and Congress are engaged in their perpetual struggle over the nation's constitutional system of checks and balances _ at a moment that seems without an exact parallel in history. By David Sanger. WITH PHOTO. (Moved in the `a' category)  MIDEAST-MELTDOWN-REVIEW (Jerusalem) _ Peace was supposed to come to Israelis and Palestinians under a process by which Israel would gradually yield territory in the West Bank and Gaza Strip, the Palestinian Authority would strive to keep Israelis safe, and the antagonists would come to understand and trust each other as they coordinated the handoffs. It was to be a self-reinforcing cycle powered by an incrementalism of ever-more-important, reciprocal concessions and tightening ties. Incrementalism turned out to be as powerful an engine as the framers of the Oslo accords envisioned in 1993. Unfortunately, it has so far proved most effective in reverse. By James Bennet. (Moved in the `i' category)  _ Also moved in the `a' category:  ENRON-LAY-INVEST-REVIEW (Undated) _ How much is enough? That was a question that came up when a lawyer for former Enron CEO Kenneth L. Lay said that Lay had been forced to repeatedly borrow millions of dollars from Enron. He needed the money, the lawyer said, because he owed money to others and had to make payments on those loans. People who can't imagine why someone with hundreds of millions of dollars would borrow money probably don't knwo how very wealthy people _ first-generation, of course _ get to that status. They don't do it by playing it safe. By Floyd Norris.  ENRON-WOMEN-REVIEW (Washington) _ A novelty about the Enron scandal is that, unlike so many other Capitol messes, it features powerful women in starring roles in a controversy that does not involve sex. Nancy Temple, Sherron Watkins, Maureen Castaneda and Martha P. Stuart all have, in one way or another, key roles in the drama. The highest-profile women in other recent scandals, Monica Lewinsky and Anita Hill, were involved with charges of sexual misconduct. A few women, such as Fawn Hall and Rose Mary Woods, figured in non-sex-related scandals like Iran-Contra and Watergate, but not in major ways. By Jill Abramson.  ALI-TYSON-REVIEW (Undated) _ Mike Tyson's style of showman-style intimidation outside the ring really began with Muhammad Ali. He turned verbal sparring into an art form that legions of admirers, from pacifists to pugilists, have tried to emulate. Part circus act, part performance art, part psych job, part street hustle, it combined to become as essential to the Ali myth as his grace in the ring. These pre-bout performances mask the sport's brutality, now personified by Tyson. While Tyson is considered by many to be boxing's nadir, the embodiment of its violence with none of its elan, Ali has continued to grow in stature. By Edward Wong.  WAR-MOVIES-REVIEW (Undated) _ War movies have always served as metaphors for America's attitude toward authority that the armed forces as institutions symbolized. Thus the respect for authority during World War II translated into respect for commanders in war movies. Later, disenchantment with authority translated into disenchantment with authority in Vietnam movies. Now, with a small group of post-Vietnam films, there is a new regard for authority but with a difference _ a difference that again reflects a changed attitude toward authority in America generally. Exhibit A is ``Black Hawk Down. The most obvious difference between this war movie and its predecessors doesn't have anything to do with authority. By Neal Gabler.  PRODUCTIVITY-POLITICS-REVIEW (Undated) _ Productivity is an economic concept that makes people's eyes glaze over. But rub them and stay awake. It is about to join taxes, budget deficits and the recession as a grass-roots political issue. When Democrats and Republicans lash out at each other over stimulus packages and deficits, productivity _ the output of the economy per hour of work _ is an invisible presence in their arguments. By Louis Uchitelle.  JOKE-SURVEY-TEXT-REVIEW (Undated) _ Further proof that all humor is local: excerpts from some of the findings of the British Association for the Advancement of Science's global online survey of jokes.  THISWEEK-REVIEW (Undated) _ Some highlights of the week.  _ Also moved in the `i' category:  GOMA-CONGO-REVIEW (Goma, Congo) _ This border city of 400,000 on Congo's eastern edge is undoubtedly one of the most miserable places on earth. Hundreds of thousands of residents outran a volcanic eruption here on Jan. 17. In Goma, disasters are a part of life. It is also refugee central, the way station for desperate Rwandans and desperate Congolese fleeing war on one side of the border or the other. By Marc Lacey. WITH MAP.  COLOMBIA-REBELS-REVIEW (Bogota) _ Many Colombians were relieved when the country's largest rebel group, the Revolutionary Armed Forces of Colombia, or FARC _ which is also the largest and richest guerrilla army in Latin American history _ unexpectedly conceded to government demands and agreed to start cease-fire talks. But they were also puzzled. Why now? By Juan Forero.  AFGHAN-PRISONERS-REVIEW (United Nations) _ Are the detainees from Afghanistan being held at Guantanamo prisoners of war? And if not, as the United States maintains, what are they? It is not just their treatment that depends on the answer, but the strength of a coalition in which many partners are becoming anxious about Washington's apparent lack of concern for international rules. By Serge Schmemann.  STORY QUESTIONS OR RERUNS:  The Weekend Supervisor is Lynn Hoogenboom. DRUMMOND AYRES Jr. c.2002 New York Times News Service  Woes Keep Piling Up for Condit Campaign  With less than six weeks to go until California's March 5 primaries, the news just keeps getting worse for Rep. Gary A. Condit.  Last week, three more top Democrats announced that they would not support his re-election effort in the state's 18th Congressional District in the upper Central Valley. All three _ Tony Coelho, who held the seat before Condit, and Dianne Feinstein and Barbara Boxer, the state's two U.S. senators _ said the fallout from the mysterious disappearance of Chandra Levy, the Capitol Hill intern with whom Condit reportedly had an affair, had ruined his chances of winning a seventh term.  ``Time to move on,'' Coelho said.  ``Time for a change,'' Feinstein said.  ``Time for a fresh start,'' Boxer said.  Then all three called on Democrats to nominate Assemblyman Dennis Cardoza on March 5, saying that of the dozen or so hopefuls competing in the primary, Cardoza, who was once on Condit's staff, was best positioned to keep the 18th District out of Republican hands.  Condit scoffed at this latest demand that he pull out of the race, declaring, ``Not going to walk away.''  He has insisted from the start of the investigation of Levy's disappearance _ an investigation that continues _ that he has no idea what happened to her. The police say that while he has not always been as responsive to their questions as they would have liked, he is nevertheless not a suspect. Still, his poll numbers continue to slump, he is struggling to raise campaign cash and old allies continue to abandon him.  On Death to Terrorists and Life in Senate  What to do with captured terrorists has become an issue in this year's U.S. Senate campaign in South Carolina.  The likely Republican nominee, Rep. Lindsey Graham, whose congressional district covers the northwest corner of the state, says execution would be the right penalty for some of them, especially any convicted by military tribunals. In a letter sent a few days ago to Defense Secretary Donald H. Rumsfeld, Graham said the Defense Department should ``allow the death penalty for convicted war criminals.''  ``Opponents of capital punishment will use every tactic at their disposal to prevent any war criminal from being sentenced to death,'' warned Graham, a former military prosecutor. ``It is my hope that the administration will not give in.''  How did that strike the likely Democratic nominee, Alex Sanders, a former legislator, judge and college president?  Deadly force can be justified in self-defense on the battlefield, Sanders told The Greenville News. But for a military tribunal to impose a death penalty, he went on, would be ``contrary to the will of God.''  South Carolina is one of the nation's most conservative and Republican states, and Sanders acknowledged in his next campaign appearance that his view of capital punishment was ``contrary to what the vast majority of South Carolina believes.'' But, he said, he has been an opponent of capital punishment all his life, even though he believes it to be constitutional.  That struck Trey Walker, Graham's campaign strategist, as a fuzzy, ``Clintonesque'' approach to the issue. The issue, he added, is sure to be ``huge'' in any Graham-Sanders contest.  From Counting Ballots to Counting Dollars  In five congressional campaigns in Florida's 13th District, just south of St. Petersburg, Rep. Dan Miller never spent more than $380,000 to win, and usually considerably less. He did not have to spend more. He is a Republican and the district is heavily Republican.  Miller will not seek a sixth term, though he could almost certainly breeze to victory again if he did. He says it is time to retire.  A number of Republicans are thinking about running for his seat. But only one is given a real shot at winning the nomination: Secretary of State Katherine Harris, she of Election 2000 ballot-counting fame, or notoriety, depending on the ideological point of view.  How much does she figure it will take to win both the primary and general elections?  Two million dollars.  Say what?  It's not easy to run as Katherine Harris, she explains, not even in a Republican-heavy district. A lot of Democrats are still angry, she says, so angry that they would like to make her ``the national punching bag.''  She figures the way to head that off is to outspend them.  Recipes for Election And Mac 'n' Cheese  Back in 2000, when James Kraft first ran for a seat in the Arizona state House of Representatives, he had a problem. Nobody knew who he was. He was new to politics.  But there was that name _ Kraft _ and he really was a bona fide member of the famous foods family.  Then it hit him.  Mac and cheese, the all-time comfort food.  He handed out 12,000 boxes of it.  He won.  Now it's election time again. Again Kraft, a Republican from Phoenix, has a problem.  Name ID.  The lines of his district have been redrawn. A lot of new people are out there who do not know who he is, and he faces at least four challengers, maybe five.  Answer: 40,000 boxes of Kraft mac and cheese. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20020126.0099 type=story > <DATELINE> HOUSTON, Texas (BC-A27MUSLIM-SIDER-HNS) </DATELINE> <TEXT> 	nyt_eng_200201.gz

ENTERTAINMENT &AMP; ARTS  (Coded `e')  ON THE COVER  MUSIC-ALICIA-KEYS (New York) _ Alicia Keys, who turns 21 on Feb. 1, has been nominated for Grammys in six categories, including best new artist and song of the year for ``Fallin'.'' In an interview, she discusses her life, her songs and her plans as she prepares for her first headlining tour which brings her to Radio City Music Hall Feb. 1. By Jon Pareles. (2,000 words)  With photo NYT32 sent on Jan. 24.  Also see MUSIC-PUBLISHER-GERSON  THEATER  THEATER-POWELL (New York) _ Decades after Dawn Powell was buried anonymously in New York City's potter's field, she is, as Gore Vidal predicted, ``on the verge of ceasing to be a cult and becoming a major religion.'' Dozens of writers and performers will participate in ``Permanent Visitor: A Festival Celebrating Dawn Powell in New York'' through March 11. The event includes performances of five plays, a play about her, stage adaptations of her short stories and performers reading from her diaries and novels _ not the touching ones about Ohio, where she was born in 1897, but the witty ones about New York, where she lived for almost half a century. She died in 1965 with virtually all her 15 novels out of print. By Jonathan Mandell. (1,700 words)  With photo NYT33 sent on Jan. 24.  THEATER-OLDIES (New York) _ George M. Cohan wrote the line ``give my regards to old Broadway.'' If he were writing it today, the emphasis would be on the ``old.'' This year's Broadway season includes one-woman shows by Beatrice Arthur, 78, Elaine Stritch, 76, and Barbara Cook, 74; new plays by Edward Albee, 73 and Neil Simon, 74; a star turn by Uta Hagen, 82, in a new play; a revival of ``Into the Woods'' by Stephen Sondheim, 71; and two revivals of plays by Arthur Miller, 86. ``We're going through a geriatric phase now,'' said Rocco Landesman, the president of Jujamcyn Theaters. By Mervyn Rothstein. (2,000 words)  With photos NYT34 and NYT35 sent on Jan. 24.  FILM  FILM-MORETTI (New York) _ Nanni Moretti writes, directs, produces and stars in his movies, using his own preoccupations and opinions as the starting point for his deadpan humor. Moretti's reputation as a satirist explains the surprise that greeted his latest film, ``La Stanza del Figlio'' (``The Son's Room'') when it came out in Europe last year. The film was praised for its austere emotional power both in Italy and in France, where it won the Palme d'Or, at the Cannes International Film Festival. The movie is Italy's submission for this year's foreign film dd Oscar. ``The Son's Room,'' which opens in New York on Feb. 1, is about a close and loving family hit by an unexpected tragedy. By Laura Winters. (2000 words)  With photo NYT36 sent on Jan. 24.  TELEVISION  TV-SMALLVILLE (Undated) _ ``Smallville'' is one of a handful of new shows this season to have attained breakout status. While the show isn't quite as glossy as ABC's ``Alias'' and hasn't revolutionized the look of television drama the way Fox's ``24'' has, ``Smallville'' peddles its own brand of American corn, which, when served with a pinch of teenage angst, a hint of paranormality and a fresh take on superman, one of the most durable icons in pop culture history, makes an immensely satisfying meal. By Hal Hinson (2,000 words)  With photo NYT37 sent on Jan. 24.  TV-BLUE-PLANET (Undated) _ Since the advent of Discovery and its spin-offs, television has become rife with nature and it is much harder to recreate the sense of wonder viewers must have felt when Jacques Cousteau began filming underwater in the 1950s. ``Blue Planet'' contains echoes of those days. The first four hourlong installments of the ``Blue Planet'' will be shown Jan. 27 and Jan. 28 on Discovery. By Julie Salamon. (1,200 words)  MUSIC  APPRECIATION-PEGGY-LEE (Undated) _ Peggy Lee taught Terry Teachout about sex. He was 12 at the time and working his way through his father's record collection that included Peggy Lee's recording of ``Fever.'' ``I got the message, and then some,'' writes Teachout. ``What the lady on the record had in mind was pretty much what I had in mind 24 hours a day, except that her point of view was more informed.'' Thirty-four years later Teachout says he now knows that ``Fever'' was the least of her. ``She was exquisite _ there is no other word for her,'' he says. By Terry Teachout. (480 words)  (See photos NYT2, NYT3 and NYT4 sent on Tuesday with the Peggy Lee obituary.)  MUSIC-DALBAVIE (Paris) _ Marc Andre Dalbavie's new ``Color,'' to be performed by Christoph Eschenbach and the Orchestre de Paris on Jan. 30 at Carnegie Hall, is a little different for him: it's a departure into normality, with the full ensemble on stage, not scattered around a concert hall. By Paul Griffiths. (1,600 words).  MUSIC-PUBLISHER-GERSON (Los Angeles) _ Unlike record producers who help make and market albums or managers who can mold an artist's image, music publishers rarely share in the credit for an artist's success. That is true, in part, because many do little beyond collecting a fee for publishing a song. But the best music publishers not only aggressively promote their artists' work but also work closely with artists' managers to nurture talent. That's the kind of publisher Jody Gerson has been for Alicia Keys. By Laura M. Holson. (1,000 words)  ART  EXHIBIT-WOMEN-CHINA-PAINTERS (New York) _ Women ruled the worlds of china painting beginning in the 1870s, when they organized in groups to do china painting. Their influence increased in the 1880s as they founded factories, which employed mostly female artists. By then, women with increased leisure time began painting china at home, responding to advertisements urging them to buy their own blanks, brushes, paints and kilns. By the 1890s, china painting was a do-it-yourself craze that women across America joined for pleasure or profit. Now the role of women in this movement is recognized with ``Women China Decorators in the Metropolitan Museum of Art,'' on view through April 14. By Rita Reif. (1,100 words)  With photo NYT38 sent on Jan. 24.  DANCE  DANCE-JONES (New York) _ As he nears 50, Bill T. Jones has shed a degree of his political vehemence and is searching for beauty and for a new home for the Bill T. Jones/Arnie Zane Dance Co. The quest for beauty has produced three new works to be performed at Alice Tully Hall beginning on Jan. 31. The search for a home is for more than a building; it is to ensure the future of his choreography and his company. By Wendy Perron. (1,800 words)  With photos NYT39 and NYT40 sent on Jan. 24. Wald (1,900 words)  CUBA-CRUISES (Undated) _ Much of the U.S. travel industry has long been eyeing Cuba as a destination, and no segment more covetously than cruise lines. Sept. 11 has made Cuba even more inviting as cruise lines added more Caribbean itineraries. Projections show show that if the ban on travel by Americans to Cuba were lifted, 750,000 American cruise passengers a year would visit Cuba in the first few years and the numbers would grow to 1.8 million in a decade, more if ferry service could be established. By Edwin McDowell. (850 words)  TRAVEL-MEXICO-QUERETARO (Undated) _ Queretaro's plazas and chapels were settings for the rise and fall of colonial power in Mexico. From here Franciscan missionaries launched campaigns in the early 16th century to force Indians into slavery and Christianity. Ignacio Perez, Mexico's Paul Revere, rode out from this city in 1810 to warn the organizers of the War of Independence of a plot to kill them. The last Mexican emperor, Archduke Maximilian, was executed here in 1867 and in 1917 in the Teatro de la Republica, Mexico's Constitution was drafted. ``If you want to walk in history,'' one guide said, ``then this is a city that has worked hard to preserve it.'' By Ginger Thompson. (1,500 words)  SHORT STORIES  TRAVEL-Q&AMP;A (Undated) _ Answers to questions about getting married on a cruise from Venice to Rome, tours associated with J.S. (900 words)  TRAVEL-NY-RITZ-CARLTON (New York) _ On Sept. 11, the luxury Ritz-Carlton chain was less than a month from opening a hotel in Battery Park City, five blocks south of the World Trade Center. The opening was delayed until Jan. 29. About two-thirds of the 298-rooms, face the water, and each of these has a telescope to take in the sights, most notably the Statue of Liberty. By Ray Cormier. Not only has terrorism cast doubts on the ability of globalization to cure all ills; globalization is perhaps the culprit behind the synchronized slowdown of the world economy, the first global downturn since the oil crisis of the 1970s. , you know,	 Word around the Bruins dressing room is that forward Andrei Nazarov, who was dealt to Phoenix Friday for a fifth-round draft pick, asked management for a trade. Nazarov had been getting little ice time in a fourth-line role and spending more time fighting than playing. He indicated he wanted to go to a team that would allow him to play regularly and not have to be an enforcer. Nazarov, 27, was a high draft pick in 1992 when he was taken by San Jose at No. 10 overall. After stops with the Sharks and Tampa Bay, Nazarov had 32 points (10 goals) in his only full season in Calgary, 1999-2000. He had a career-high 12 goals in 1996-97 with San Jose. Word is he would like to put up numbers like that again instead of putting up his dukes. His departure means more pressure on Boston's newest folk hero, P.J. Stock. Although the Bruins have plenty of players who can stick up for themselves, coach Robbie Ftorek would rather Bill Guerin, Sean O'Donnell, Joe Thornton etc., stay out of the penalty box. When asked about the likelihood of his duties expanding, Stock shrugged. ``Oh well,'' he said, feigning nonchalance but clearly loving the possibilities. ``Ding, ding.'' . . . Ken Hitchcock's firing in Dallas was a long time coming. Despite consistently good results (a Stanley Cup title, five straight division crowns) over the six years he was behind the bench, his shelf life had expired. His players were no longer listening. So, how is it that Scotty Bowman has managed to stay behind the Red Wings' bench for nine years? Easy. He doesn't talk to his players . . . Think the Devils wish all their games could be in South Florida? Guess he'll be wearing a new one now . . . Give Edmonton forward Todd Marchant full marks for facing the music after coach Craig MacTavish benched him for the second period of Wednesday's game against Colorado. MacTavish, who was traded to the Rangers from Edmonton for Marchant in March 1994, hasn't been happy all year with the way Marchant has played. ``He said I bring three things to the table: speed, grit, and heart,'' said Marchant. ``But since the beginning of the season, I hadn't brought grit and heart to the games.'' Marchant said he was upset with himself for his play on a goal by Colorado defenseman Adam Foote. After Marchant lost the draw, he said he ``flamingoed,'' lifting one foot to avoid getting hit by the shot. ``I didn't consciously do it,'' he said. ``It was embarrassing to do something like that.
