, they say,	 Jermaine O'Neal, Duncan, Kidd, and Antonio McDyess are believed to be the best of the bunch who can be free agents in the summer of 2003. Alas, there's a problem. League executives believe that, despite the new TV deal, the salary cap will go down next season for the first time since its implementation in 1983. It's at $42.5 million this season. A lot of the teams anticipating big cap space next summer (Orlando, Miami) were hoping for a cap in the high 40s. That seems highly unlikely now. In addition, teams now feel there won't be a luxury tax for this season, but there is certain to be one for 2002-03. That doesn't bode well for the Celtics, who are determined not to pay a tax but already are perilously close to the presumed threshold without factoring in any draft picks or re-signed free agents . . . The Bulls went to great lengths to distance themselves from the Great Lights Out controversy last weekend prior to Jordan's first game at the United Center as a Wizard. Jordan was close to tears during a 70-second standing ovation that was still going strong when the house lights went off for the introduction of the Bulls. The thinking at the time was that Jordan was being cut short by his former team. But the Bulls say the decision to turn off the lights and go on with the pregame program came from NBC, not them. Written to acknowledge the 10th anniversary of Bartok's death, it begins, much like Bartok's Music for Strings, Percussion and Celeste, with a subdued fugue for strings, builds to an anguished apotheosis, then subsides back into the quizzical opening fugue. , he thought,	 Saturday night's program began with a remarkably lucid, flowing and never lugubrious account of Strauss' autumnal ``Metamorphosen'' for 23 Solo Strings. This 1945 work, which borrows from the theme of the funeral march of Beethoven's ``Eroica'' Symphony, was Strauss' elegy to the end of the heroic German tradition, indeed, to the end, he thought, of German culture as he had known it. So, it provided a fitting prelude to Beethoven's Ninth, visionary music that defined that heroic ideal. </P> 	nyt_eng_200201.gz

Business Day Front Page of The New York Times, Monday, Jan. 28.  (Lede)  HP-ITANIUM (Undated) _ Hewlett-Packard argues that Itanium-based server computers are its future, a way for it to catch up to IBM and Sun in the big systems business _ making and servicing Itanium servers, mostly running NT. HP, which jointly developed the Itanium chip with Intel, has first dibs on a lot of the Itanium technology. Walter Hewlett, the renegade Hewlett-Packard board member, thinks this is a fool's errand, based on his view that ``Microsoft is going to win everything.'' By Steve Lohr.  (Scheduled to move by 7 p.m. ET.)  (Off lede)  ENRON-ANDERSEN (Undated) _ Enron's collapse is forcing Arthur Andersen, the company's auditor, to make hard decisions about its strategy, including whether to sell off its consulting practice and how to reassure and retain partners overseas. Analysts and executives at one consulting firm that is a potential buyer for Andersen's consulting practice said that Andersen was at least exploring that option with consulting firms and computer manufacturers. By Jonathan D. Glater and Edward Wyatt.  (Scheduled to move by 8 p.m. ET.)  (Eds: This story will move in the `a' category.)  (Top center)  ENRON-DOCTOR (Houston) _ In the five years since Dr. John Mendelsohn, a respected cancer researcher, became president of the University of Texas M.D. Anderson Cancer Center and began courting wealthy donors, politicians and volunteers, the center's research budget has grown 71 percent and the number of patients has increased 49 percent. But now, at what should be the pinnacle of his career, Mendelsohn finds himself in the middle of accusations surrounding two companies: Enron, whose collapse is the subject of intense scrutiny by Congress and regulators, and ImClone Systems, a biotechnology company that is being accused of misleading investors about the possible approval of a drug that Mendelsohn was instrumental in developing. Mendelsohn serves on the boards of both companies. By Jo Thomas with Reed Abelson.  With graphic.  (Scheduled to move by 7 p.m. ET.)  (Eds: This story will move in the `a' category.)  (Bottom right)  NFL-PAY-TV (Undated) _ The shifting dynamics of the pay television market _ satellite TV and cable _ may best be illustrated by NFL football games. Nearly a decade ago, assuming that satellite TV was merely a niche market, the NFL granted the satellite company DirecTV an exclusive package of all games _ while broadcast television and cable was restricted to local games and selected games from other cities. Now, with EchoStar in a pending deal to acquire DirecTV, which would create the nation's second-largest pay television company, the NFL package is starting to look like a major asset _ one that cable companies covet. By Seth Schiesel.  With graphic.  (Story has moved.)  (Bottom center)  NEW-REPUBLIC-MEDIA (Undated) _ A look at The New Republic magazine. By David D. Kirkpatrick.  (Scheduled to move by 9 p.m. ET.)  Here are tonight's columns and regular features:  NEW-ECON-COLUMN (Undated) _ Enron, which made its initially good name as a new-economy innovator employing computer networks to create and trade complex new types of commodity contracts, failed for many reasons other than its underlying business model. In fact, what Enron did best may still suggest a model for other innovative companies in the future. The New Economy Column. By Daniel Altman.  (Column has moved.)  PATENTS (Undated) _ Enron's future as a business enterprise is uncertain, but its name could live on for 10 years _ as a trademark, at least. Enron moved a step closer to winning a trademark for its tilted ``E'' logo last week when the U.S. Office of Patents and Trademarks published the company's application in the office's Official Gazette. The Patents column. By Sabra Chartrand.  (Column has moved.)  COMPUTER-BRIEFS (Undated) _ A software executive helps the FBI foil an effort by a rival's disgruntled employee to sell the rival's customer database ... Palm has a new product, a long-delayed answer to the BlackBerry.  (Column has moved.)  E-COMMERCE-COLUMN (Undated) _ IBM and Compaq have been making significant progress in their efforts to sell computers and related products via the Internet. And while they aren't yet close to unseating the direct-sales leader, they are doing enough to move their companies substantially ahead of where they were a year or two ago. By Bob Tedeschi.  With photo.  (Column has moved.)  VACCINE-AD-COLUMN (Undated) _ It costs only $30 to immunize an infant against preventable diseases like measles and mumps, according to the Vaccine Fund, a worldwide organization that raises money for universal immunization. "105643"	"he"	"wrote"	" Westermann's sculpture is often seen as a link between the lyric boxes of Joseph Cornell and the menacing plush toys of Mike Kelley. His sensibilities were shaped to a large extent by his experiences as a gunner on aircraft carriers during World War II. In a letter home, he described a sister ship, the USS Franklin, after it was attacked by kamikaze pilots, ``engulfed in flames, smoke, and explosions from stern to stern.'' In all, 724 crewmen were killed. Weeks later, he wrote, the Franklin was ``still smoking &AMP; had a terrific list &AMP; the smell of death from her was horrible.'' Clark, a biologist at Harvard who heads an international effort to build a scientific foundation for such a shift. The N.Y. Times News Service night supervisor is Pat Ryan; phone: (888) 346-9867; e-mail: patryan(at)nytimes.com.  INTERNATIONAL  (Will move in ``i'' news file.)  MIDEAST-NIDAL (Jerusalem) _ Abu Nidal, the Palestinian whose name became a byword for international terrorism in the 1970s and 1980s, was found dead three days ago in his Baghdad apartment with multiple gunshot wounds, according to a Palestinian newspaper. So far, however, there has been no confirmation. By Serge Schmemann.  RUSSIA-HELICOPTER (Moscow) _ A Russian military helicopter, the largest kind in the world, crash-lands near an airbase in Grozny, Chechnya, with more than 110 on board. Information is filtering out slowly. About 35 soldiers are in the hospital; the remainder, about 85, are unaccounted for at present. It is possible they were killed in the crash-landing. The pilot, who survived, was interviewed by Russian media. By his account, he heard a loud noise in the right engine, got a warning light, and requested emergency permission to land. Some accounts say that he landed in a minefield _ a real possibility, given the proximity of the airbase. Chechen rebels claim they shot it down, but the Russian Defense Ministry said the problem was a fire in the engine. By Steven Lee Myers.  With a map.  POPE-POLAND (Krakow, Poland) _ Pope John Paul II said an emotional goodbye on Monday to countrymen who insisted among themselves that he would live to come back another time. By Frank Bruni.  NATIONAL GENERAL  (Will move in ``a'' news file.)  NC-KORAN-STUDIES (Chapel Hill, N.C.) _ After the attacks of Sept. 11, colleges across the country were rushing to teach more about Islam. But as students return to campus this year, a fight over a summer reading assignment at the University of North Carolina is proving how difficult tolerance can be. Freshmen had been assigned a book about the Koran and will meet Monday to discuss it. But the assignment has led to court battles, with a conservative Christian group charging that the assignment represents ``forced Islamic indoctrination.'' The state Senate is debating a bill that would deny aid to the university unless it gives equal time to ``all known religions.'' By Kate Zernike.  AMTRAK-ACELA (Dateline TK) _ Amtrak put nine of its Acela Express passenger trains back into duty in the Northeast Corridor on Monday, but the railway's president said it will be days before the high-speed service returns to full strength. Repair work continued on nine other Acela Express trains. All 18 were sidelined most of last week after inspectors found cracks in shock-absorbing assemblies beneath many of the locomotives.  NY-POLICE-RESPONSE (New York) _ The city's police and fire commissioners said Monday that they would introduce wide-ranging changes that are intended to foster better coordination between their departments during catastrophic events like the attack on the World Trade Center. By Al Baker.  SCIENCE  (Will move in ``a'' news file.)  This week's Science Times is devoted to the environment, under the rubric Managing Planet Earth. Articles in the section examine issues that will be discussed at next week's World Summit on Sustainable Development, sponsored by the United Nations in Johannesburg, South Africa.  SCI-EARTH-MANAGE (Undated) _ Nearly 70 years ago, Vladimir I. Vernadsky, a Soviet geochemist, reflecting on his world, made a startling observation: Through technology and sheer numbers, he wrote, people were becoming a geological force, shaping the planet's future just as rivers and earthquakes had shaped its past. Aided by satellites and supercomputers, and mobilized by the evident environmental damage of the last century, humans have a real chance to begin balancing economic development with sustaining Earth's ecological webs, said Dr. William C. Clark, a biologist at Harvard who heads an international effort to build a scientific foundation for such a shift. That prospect, however slim, is attracting more than 100 world leaders and thousands of other participants to the U.N. World Summit on Sustainable Development, which starts on Monday in Johannesburg. No matter what they come up with, ice ages, volcanoes and shifting tectonic plates will dwarf human activities in the long run. But communities and countries face concrete choices in the next decade that are likely to determine the quality of human life and the environment well into the 22nd century. By Andrew C. Revkin.  With photo and graphic.  CULTURE  (Will move in ``e'' news file.)  NY-THEATER-OUTLOOK (New York) _ Doomsayers have good reason to be concerned about the health of the theater in New York City _ a precipitous decline in advance ticket sales, a host of longtime hits posting closing notices, the usual September doldrums around the corner exacerbated by this year's 9/11 anniversary. But just as producers girded themselves for the worst after Sept. 11 only to see Broadway set attendance records, the last two weeks have shown a similar resilience. And this time it has extended far outside the theater district, from the Lower East Side up to Harlem. By Robin Pogrebin.  SPORTS  (Will move in ``s'' news file.)  BBO-LABOR (New York) _ Baseball labor developments, as the two sides meet again with less than two weeks before a strike date. By Murray Chass. "137469"	"he"	"wrote"	" Story Filed </P> </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20030421.0036 type=advis > <HEADLINE> AMATEUR VIDEO HELPS NASA TRACK SHUTTLE'S LAST MINUTES </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> HOUSTON  (BC-SCI-SHUTTLE-PHOTOS-AR) </DATELINE> <TEXT> Dan McNew thought he had shot the home movie of a lifetime. He had aimed his digital video camera at the shuttle Columbia as it returned to Earth on Feb. 1; living near Dallas, in the path of the returning shuttle, he had caught the sight on video several times before.  When he viewed the images in light of the shuttle's loss, he thought he might have captured a crucial moment. He read about the shuttle's yawing spin before breakup and sent an e-mail message to a reporter.  ``That description matches perf%ctMy with what I caught on video that morning,'' he wrote. He had zoomed in on the shuttle as it hurtled by and captured it flying sideways, he wrote, adding, ``I believe I am the only one who has a clear close-up video of this event.''  He had given a copy of his videotape to NASA two days after the accident, and the space agency came back and bought his camera so that researchers could determine whether the digital images stored in ones and zeros on the tape showed what McNew claimed. And he waited to hear whether he had made history.  In the video nation, almost no moment goes untaped, and anyone might be the next Abraham Zapruder, whose jumpy images of the assassination of President John F. Kennedy provided invaluable evidence to a generation of investigators (and conspiracy theorists).  The nearly ubiquitous cameras grab images of mothers slapping their children in parking lots and Rodney King being beaten _ and, as it turns out, the space shuttle Columbia during its descent. Some 3,000 people contacted NASA in the days after the shuttle disaster to offer their firsthand reports, still photographs and videos of the shuttle's entry into the atmosphere. Ultimately, some 12,000 videos and images streamed in.  For weeks, researchers combed through the photographs and videos, going to great lengths to verify that each was what its donor claimed that it was. Some turned out to be hoaxes, and a few were not even pictures of the shuttle. The researchers then culled the best and stitched together a video narrative of the flight.  And it has paid off: The images have provided a trove of data that has helped investigators piece together what happened in the final minutes of the flight.  NASA and the independent board investigating the accident have spliced video images together to tell the story of the shuttle's final descent, showing when and where major pieces of debris came off the orbiter.  The imagery has helped NASA guide debris searchers, and has helped to show that the shuttle was shedding parts before the signs of serious trouble appeared on sensors that could be read by mission control on the ground.  The process of determining which videos provided the most useful information fell to a team drawn largely from NASA's Image Science and Analysis Group, at the Johnson Space Center, which has long analyzed images of spacecraft during missions.  These analysts are practiced in enhancing images to bring out details that may otherwise go unnoticed.  The wroup hqs usut0vhabout 80 seconds after liftoff, and has even aided police in criminal investigations based on photographic evidence.  The team painstakingly analyzed every still photo and video, checking to see whether they were altered in any way. In some cases the investigators even bought the cameras from peo`l%(who had particularly promising footage, like McNew, to see if they could re-create the conditions under which the video was shot, to check colors to make sure they were accurate, and to figure out how to sharpen the pictures more effectively. ``Any footage that we can obtain is important to NASA,'' said a member of the video team.  The team's leader, Paul Hil,,  missions operation director for the space shuttle, presented the video results last month to the independent board investigating the shuttle disaster. He said all but about a minute or two of the shuttle's path over the United States had been filmed. (There ms0c gap between Albuquerque, N.M., and Dallas, which Hill said was not surprising, considering the sparse population in that area.)  (BEGIN OPTIONAL TRIM)  On the videos, flashes and bright dots of debris leaving the orbiter gave important clues to when and where pieces of the shuttle were lost on its way back to Earth, and to help identify which ``debris events'' seemed most important. A bright flash of 6 to 12 seconds and signs of an object breaking away from the shuttle led NASA to send debris recovery teams to Caliente, Nev., to search for the object, called Debris No. 6, that was tracked by radar on the way down. So far, nothing has been recovered from that snow-covered area, but searches are expected to continue.  The brightest flash occurred over Arizona, and the flash persisted for nearly 8 seconds, depending on which video is viewed. That flash, and the image of bright dots falling away, was called Debris No. 14.  The NASA video team used some ingenious techniques to fix the time of the video bits, sometimes relying on celestial clues. The video of the Debris No. 6 flash, for example, showed the shuttle flying in front of Venus. ``When our flight dynamics folks saw this, they were very excited because this allowed us to put this video within plus or minus a second,'' Hill told the independent commission investigating the incident. Because they knew the location of the person making the video, the research team was able to use astronomical data to establish the precise moment that the video was shot.  The teams are also analyzing the images in hopes of gleaning spectroscopic data from the flashes, to determine precisely what was burning in any given flash _ whether aluminum or silica or the reinforced carbon-carbon composite that makes up the leading edge of the shuttle's wings.  Hill had high praise for the volunteer videographers. ``These people are definitely our heroes,'' he said.  Not everyone was so heroic, however. Some of the early submissions were hoaxes, and researchers spend valuable time having to deb unk them. ``They have been able to sort out the hoaxes and the false images and the artifacts from the things that are real,'' said Doug White, an official at the NASA contractor United Space Alliance, who has been working with the video team.  Some were laughably inept, like the video submitted to NASA that clearly showed a bright spot moving across the screen, but turned out to be cars driving down the road past the camera with their headlights on. Then there were the widely circulated photos billed as Israeli satellite images of Columbia exploding in space. They were actually taken from the 1998 movie ``Armageddon.''  Other people with video had profit in mind. ``There have been two isolated cases out West of two individuals who strung us to collect on the Columbia gravy train,'' Hill told members of the board.  (END OPTIONAL TRIM)  In the case of McNew, NASA concluded that he had not, in fact, captured a video image of the shuttle flying sideways. Video analysis showed that the camera distorted the image, producing a visual halo that made it appear several times the size it would be if the picture had been clear.  Still, said John Ira Petty, a spokesman for NASA, ``We really are grateful to these people; we need all of the information we can get.''  McNew said he was disappointed, but happy to have helped in some way. ``It was exciting to think that I possibly had something,'' he said. ``My new motto is, `Life is unpredictable, so keep a camera nearby and catch it.' "140607"	"he"	"wrote"	" The writer is writer at large at Esquire magazine. </P> </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20040731.0036 type=multi > <HEADLINE> NEWS OF THE WEEK IN REVIEW (THE WEEK IN REVIEW </HEADLINE> <TEXT> THE DEMOCRATS' WEEK IN BOSTON  THE MESSAGE - STRENGTH: The Democrats methodically sought to counter President Bush's advantage among voters as the commander in chief in the war on terrorism. Sen. John Kerry, in his acceptance speech Thursday night, used the word strength 17 times -- and that was after he pronounced himself as reporting for duty. As he strode to the podium at the FleetCenter, a flashing electronic sign read simply A Strong America.  A word-frequency count of the speeches delivered during the first three days of the conventions showed that strong and strength were used 141 times. Hope came in a distant, but strong, second, with 98 mentions. Values came in third at 89.  THE ISSUES: The same word-frequency counts showed that health care (161 mentions) and jobs (127) are the issues the Democrats think will help them win in November.  THE WINNERS: Other than Kerry and Sen. John Edwards, the big winners of the convention were Barack Obama and Bill Clinton.  Clinton may not be a candidate, but Elvis, as he is sometimes called, is clearly his party's star. Among other things in his speech on Monday night, he thanked the Republicans for pursuing tax policies that allow him to keep so much of the money he's been making since leaving the White House.  Tuesday was Obama's turn. A 42-year-old candidate for the Senate from Illinois, Obama is handsome, a graduate of Harvard and Columbia and the child of a black African father and a white American mother. And if he went to the podium a political youngster, he came back a star.  A week ago, he said later, people were calling me Alabama and 'yo mama.' But after his audacity of hope speech, those days seemed long gone.  THE ENEMY OF MY ENEMY: I'm using them. We can use each other. So said Ron Reagan, son of the late Republican President Ronald Reagan, about the Democratic leaders who gave him time to speak Tuesday in favor of embryonic stem cell research, and against those in the Bush administration who oppose such research.  NOT READY FOR PRIME TIME: An estimated 15,000 journalists showed up in Boston, dwarfing the number of delegates, but the major TV networks restricted their coverage to one hour a night, for three of the four nights. Gerald M. Rafshoon, who was Jimmy Carter's television adviser, called the networks' decision a complete disgrace, but the networks and their news anchors defended their decision by saying that the conventions were not news, but scripted campaign events. Perhaps more to the point, network viewership for these events has been in steady decline since 1992.  MAKING THE MOST OF THE MOMENT: Conventions usually serve up large quantities of red-meat oratory. This year, however, Kerry put out the word that speakers were to accentuate the positive and to avoid angry partisanship. (John Edwards, in his speech, never even mentioned Bush.) Almost everyone followed orders, but not Al Sharpton.  On Wednesday, Sharpton, who is said to have promised convention organizers that he would speak for seven minutes, went on for 20, delivering a largely improvised attack on Bush and the Republicans. I suggest to you tonight that if George Bush had selected the court in '54, Clarence Thomas would have never got to law school, Sharpton said to the cheering delegates in one of a set of high-energy digs.  SECRET STEREOTYPES: The National Journal reported that it had learned the code names that the Secret Service and others will be using for Kerry, Edwards and Teresa Heinz Kerry. Kerry, the Bostonian, is Minuteman. John Edwards, from the part of the country where NASCAR reigns, is Speedway. More perplexingly, Heinz Kerry is Mahogany.  THE BIG FINISH: At the end of John Kerry's speech, a sea of red, white and blue balloons were to cascade to the convention floor, but some failed to drop. The convention's executive producer, Don Mischer, frantically began asking, 'What's happening with the balloons, we need more balloons, we need all of them coming down, before uttering an epithet recently employed by the vice president. Sadly for Mischer, millions of CNN viewers were eavesdropping at the time. The network anchor apologized.  IF CLINTON COULD RUN AGAIN . . .  At the convention last week, former President Bill Clinton was embraced ecstatically by the Democratic faithful. That led some Democrats to wonder: What would happen if Clinton was running instead of John Kerry? Would he beat President Bush?  Not only do I think Clinton would win, but I think he would win by a substantial margin, said Larry J. Sabato, director of the Center for Politics at the University of Virginia. Given a choice between what happened under Clinton in the '90s and what has happened under Bush's tenure, there is no doubt the people would choose the Clinton conditions.  Few political strategists imagine that a Bush-Clinton contest would be a typical issue-driven race.  These two presidents stir the passions of supporters and detractors, political strategists said, and the election would ultimately become a battle over trust.  I think it would be a very competitive race, said David Axelrod, a Democratic political consultant in Chicago. Having said that, there is no doubt President Clinton engendered strong feelings in people and that hasn't probably changed, so it would be a dogfight.  A political consultant to Bush, Charles Black, said the challenge for Bush would be similar to what it is now -- to convince the American people that he is on the right track. And that, Black said, would be easier against Clinton because of his personal scandals.  Clinton's a better campaigner than Kerry, Black said. But he is a completely polarizing figure, and, by the time he left office, most of the independent voters outside the Democratic base were embarrassed about his personal escapades and were happy to see him go.  Douglas Schoen, a longtime pollster for Clinton, however, dismissed the notion of Clinton fatigue. While undeniably Mr. Clinton has some negatives, when he left office two-thirds of the people were satisfied with his job performance.  The big issue of today, the war on terror, could cut both ways, political analysts said. There are stark ideological differences, from Clinton's focus on treaties and coalitions with other countries, to Bush's more go-it-alone strategy.  I think the single fundamental difference between the two candidates would be their view of the world and America's role in the world, a former political director to Clinton, Douglas Sosnik, said. Bush appears to be one of the few people in the world who does not see the world as interconnected.  Of course, thanks to the Constitution, the Bush vs. Clinton fantasy will never be tested, allowing strategists to debate the possibilities until well past November.  A Republican political strategist, Frank Luntz, said that he went to the Democratic National Convention certain that the nation suffered Clinton fatigue and that, if faced with the choice, would surely pick the sitting president over the former president. He left with a different view.  I think it would be really too close to call, Luntz said. People really do look back on that Clinton era with a sense of fondness of how easy things were -- easy money, easy existence and easygoing president.  THE CAMPAIGN ROLLS OUT: President Bush and John Kerry, the new Democratic presidential nominee, will both be out on the campaign trail. Kerry and his running mate, John Edwards, are continuing their 15-day, 22-state, 3,500-mile Believe in America campaign trek, which they began on Friday, the day after the convention.  Bush, meanwhile, will divide his time between Washington and the campaign trail, visiting battleground states like Iowa, Minnesota and Michigan before heading to a wedding in Kennebunkport, Maine, over the weekend.  - COURTNEY C. RADSCH  STATE OF THE ECONOMY: Ever since the Federal Reserve began obsessing over the condition of the labor market last year, job growth has become one of the most important statistics in the economy.  July's employment report, due out Friday, is even more important than usual. For two reasons. First, job growth slowed dramatically in June. Then, last Friday, the government reported a sharp drop in consumer spending that put the brakes on the economic expansion in the second quarter.  So this Friday's report will either confirm what most forecasters want to believe -- that the spring slowdown was just a temporary wobble and America is back on the growth path -- or foreshadow a more deeply troubled economy.  - EDUARDO PORTER  REVIVING LIVE ACTION: When Collateral opens on Friday, DreamWorks SKG is hoping that the star appeal of Tom Cruise, who plays a contract killer, and Jamie Foxx, as the cabdriver who drives him around Los Angeles, will draw moviegoers.  DreamWorks SKG announced two weeks ago that it would spin off its animation unit in an initial public offering. This came soon after the opening of the animated Shrek 2, which has already earned $431 million and could end up the year's biggest hit.  By contrast, DreamWorks' live-action division has had middling success this year after a disappointing 2003. The Terminal, the Tom Hanks film, has brought in $75 million, less than what many in Hollywood expected, given its $60 million budget.  - LAURA M. HOLSON  MEETING MISS LIBERTY: The Statue of Liberty reopens to the public on Tuesday after being closed since Sept. 11, 2001. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20040731.0037 type=advis > <HEADLINE> ATTN SPORTS EDITORS: </HEADLINE> <TEXT> Sports stories from The New York Times News Service for Sunday, Aug. 1.  COMMENTARY  BBN-ROBERTS-COLUMN (Undated) -- Sports of The Times on Randy Johnson. By Selena Roberts.  BOX-REGULATION (Undated) -- Some critics call professional boxing the best example of Reaganomics in the sports business. State commissions set up their own local governing bodies, which oversee everything from safety standards to financial deals; there is no federal oversight. Unions have never gained any footing despite numerous efforts by boxers to organize themselves and by advocates to organize for them. And individual stars, whose big paydays distort boxing's overall economics, drive purses and television contracts. In short, boxing is the antithesis of the relatively socialistic economics of the National Football League. Surprisingly, it takes two pugnacious Republicans to push for the federal legislation that may help save the haggard fight game. By Gregory Jordan.  GLF-BUICK-OPEN (Grand Blanc, Mich.) -- Buick Open: third round. By Clifton Brown.  RAC-SARATOGA (Saratoga Springs, N.Y.) -- The Diana Handicap. By Bill Finley.  BBO-RICKEY-EQUATION (Undated) -- Fifty years ago, readers of Life magazine opened their Aug. 2, 1954, issue to a sprawling feature titled Goodby to Some Old Baseball Ideas, with Branch Rickey standing professorially at a faux blackboard, pointing at his heretofore secret equation, which, he wrote, reveals some new and startling truths about the nature of the game. That vertiginous equation was as alarming as it was befuddling to an audience quite content with batting average, thank you. But as preposterous as it still appears on its face, the formula, and the long article Rickey wrote to explain it, actually used concepts that some modern major-league clubs are still learning to appreciate. The night supervisor for The New York Times News Service is Pat Ryan; phone: 888-346-9867 or 212-556-1927; e-mail: patryannytimes.com.  IRAQ (Najaf, Iraq) -- Developments. An uneasy peace settled over Najaf on Friday as guerrillas loyal to the insurgent cleric Muqtada al-Sadr streamed out of the Imam Ali Shrine before a cordon of American troops, ceding control of the Shiite holy site to the mainstream religious leader, Grand Ayatollah Ali al-Sistani, and appearing to end a bloody three-week standoff. By Dexter Filkins.  With photos; see the separate IRAQ-NAJAF-PHOTO-ADVISORY.  Early ledes have moved.  IRAQ-NAJAF-SCENE (Najaf, Iraq) -- The wild dogs of Najaf ate well this week. In this holy city, in lightless basements, in empty crypts, in the shadow of the golden dome of the shrine of Imam Ali, thousands of men have tried desperately and often successfully to kill one another. They have fought with knives and guns, grenades and mortars, tanks and mines and roadside bombs, and sometimes their bare hands. Now, as a cease-fire halts the three-week fight between American forces and Iraqi insurgents, the toll from the battle is only too clear. On Friday afternoon, the decomposed bodies of insurgent fighters lay in houses in and around the Old City, which surrounds the shrine. By Alex Berenson.  RUSSIA-CRASH (Moscow) -- Developments. Russia's security service announced Friday that investigators had found traces of explosives in the wreckage of one of the two passenger airliners that crashed simultaneously on Tuesday night, indicating that it crashed because of a terrorist act. Investigators also said they were looking into the backgrounds of two women passengers with Chechen names -- one on each plane -- whose relatives have not come forward. By Steven Lee Myers.  An early lede has moved.  CHINA-ASIA (Newman, Australia) -- Not long ago Australia and China regarded each other with suspicion. But through newfound diplomatic finesse and the seemingly irresistible lure of its long economic expansion, Beijing has skillfully turned around relations with Australia, America's staunchest ally in the region. The turnabout is just one sign of the broad new influence Beijing has accumulated across the Asia Pacific with American friends and foes alike. From the mines of Newman -- an outpost of 3,000 in a corner of the outback otherwise dotted by eucalyptus and kangaroos -- to the prized forests of Myanmar, the former Burma, China's rapid growth is sucking up resources and pulling the region's varied economies in its wake. The effect is unlike anything since the rise of Japanese economic power after World War II. For now, China's presence mostly translates into money, and the doors it opens. But more and more, China is leveraging its economic clout to support its political preferences. By Jane Perlez.  With graphic.  FLA-ELECT (West Palm Beach, Fla.) - Theresa LePore, brushing away tears as she sat for an interview on a hectic morning in the Palm Beach County elections office, wanted to be clear: her contact lens was bothering her, nothing more. I'm not crying, you know, she said with a faint smile. And so she was not, even though four years after her county found itself at the red-hot center of the 2000 presidential election recount, LePore is facing perhaps even more pressure than back then, when camera crews from as far away as Japan camped at her office for weeks and more than once, her pale eyes did well up in public. She designed the infamous butterfly ballot, and so in this county that bears the most scar tissue from 2000, her name figures prominently in the rallying cries this campaign season. By Abby Goodnough.  With photo and graphic.  PUBLIC-HOUSING (Undated) - In one of the most comprehensive changes in housing policy in years, the Bush administration is scrapping the nation's three-decade-old financing system for public housing in favor of a private-sector model that will redistribute billions of dollars, chiefly from big, urban areas in the Northeast and Midwest to small, rural places in the South. By David W. Chen.  PRISON-ABUSE-RUMSFELD (Washington) -- Defense Secretary Donald H. Rumsfeld continues to maintain that a small group of rogue military police officers were responsible for most of the abuse of Iraqi detainees at the Abu Ghraib prison, contrary to the findings of two major military investigations released this week. In an interview with a Phoenix radio station late Thursday, Rumsfeld said, I have not seen anything thus far that says that the people abused were abused in the process of interrogating them or for interrogation purposes. But both the independent panel, appointed by Rumsfeld and headed by James R. Schlesinger, the former defense secretary, and a separate inquiry by three Army generals concluded that military intelligence personnel committed many of the offenses, including some in interrogations. Asian and European points: contact Philippe Hertzberg in Paris for details and prices, phone: 47-42-17-11; FAX: 47-42-80-44; e-mail: phertzbergnytimes.com. Latin American points: contact Isabel Amorim Sicherle in Sao Paulo, phone/fax: 55-11-3023-3331; e-mail: sicheianytimes.com.) Jack Rosenthal, president of The New York Times Company Foundation, was a senior editor of The Times for 26 years. Daniel Okrent, the public editor, is on vacation. The public editor serves as the readers' representative. His opinions and conclusions are his own. His address is Public Editor, The New York Times, 229 W. 43rd St., New York, N.Y. 10036-3959; or e-mail: publicnytimes.com. Telephone messages: (212) 556-7652. The public editor's column appears at least twice monthly and his Web journal can be found at nytimes.com/danielokrent. In response to my two columns during Daniel Okrent's vacation this month, readers expressed strong views about what belongs on Page 1, whether news analysis articles can truly be neutral and how to keep track of news that grows old before its time.  In line with Dan's sensible standard for choosing letters, the following present views contrary to those expressed in the columns. It is nevertheless worth noting that several present and former New York Times editors endorse the idea of a formal way to track important stories otherwise lost in the swirl of all news all the time.  - JACK ROSENTHAL  WHAT BELONGS ON PAGE 1?  In What Belongs on the Front Page of The New York Times (Aug. 22), you say that Times editors have taken pains to see that analysis, or Q-head articles, do not express opinion. That is fundamentally impossible.  To analyze something, there must be a framework or worldview. The reporters' and editors' implicit worldviews give The Times its cultural and political character: elite and left-leaning.  Given that analysis is opinion, it is worrisome that The Times now encourages writers to interpolate more analysis into their primary reporting rather than ask readers to consult a separate article.  One can aspire to neutrality in the news pages (although the decisions about what is important already introduce bias), but neutrality in analysis is an oxymoron.  - DAN CULLEN  Princeton, N.J.  If The Times really believes that its Q-head articles are not opinionated, it appears to be further down the Yellow Brick Road than a careful reading of the paper normally discloses.  The Times, you wrote, now encourages writers to interpolate more analysis into their primary reporting. But analysis or interpretation is defined and colored by the writer's point of view, which is, after all, his opinion. It is on the road to Oz.  What belongs on the front page? There's no better lesson than the Aug. 22 paper. What do we find on Page 1? A three-column headline, Good Grief! That Favorite Comic Strip Is Missing, with eye-catching graphics.  In contrast, deep inside the front section is Study: Intelligence on Qaida Money Lacking. It describes a study released by the 9/11 commission just before closing down that gave details about the financing of al-Qaida and evaluated the progress (and lack of progress) by the Justice Department in tracking and curbing that financing.  Objectively, which article is worthier of front-page coverage?  You say that what readers feel ought to be on the front page often reflects their politics, geography or age. I don't think my personal bias has anything to do with thinking that news about the 9/11 terrorists and the competence of the Bush administration's anti-terrorist efforts deserves higher priority than a feature about comic strips -- especially with the presidential election less than three months away.  - GLORIA LEITNER  Arlington, Mass.  You gave an insight into the strictly technical problems editors face in producing the front page, but didn't address the substance of the issue: What values do editors use in determining the importance of a story?  You say that readers ask why, for instance, does a paper that made much of Whitewater, a real estate deal that occurred before Bill Clinton's presidency, now fail to give Page 1 prominence to the financial improprieties at Halliburton under Dick Cheney?  You pose the question, but you never answer it.  Whether a story is featured on Page 1 or buried on Page 18 makes all the difference in the world.  - KURT KELMAN  Great Neck, N.Y.  You note that readers ask why the Whitewater story was prominently on the front page and the financial improprieties at Halliburton under Dick Cheney were not.  But by posing the question without comment, and by not clarifying the distinction between the two, you allow the idea to persist that the two situations are similar and of equal importance journalistically. They are not.  Please address the larger issue: the politically slanted content of the articles themselves.  - THORNTON SANDERS  Charlottesville, Va.  You make the point that the front page now incorporates a wider variety of topics that were once only inside The Times: Women, for example, were once catered to -- patronized, one might say today -- inside The Times with the Four F's, a daily page titled food, fashion, family and furnishings. Now, topics like diet, divorce and retail rivalries regularly win front-page attention.  This attempt to defend the female readership makes me wonder if we really have moved beyond patronizing. Perhaps it would be important to recognize that women's areas of interest, like the front page, are also more diverse.  - SARA KIPPUR  Cambridge, Mass.  I liked the column, until you told us that The Times is famously scrupulous about language use. One of the faults I find in the language of The Times these days is the unnecessary use, day after day, and sometimes page after page, of famously. What does it add? The word serves no real purpose except to annoy.  At least you didn't use that other in-word beloved of so many copycat writers: arguably.  What to Do When News Grows Old Before Its Time (Aug. 8) struck a nerve with respect to the lack of good follow-up done by newspapers.  But the solutions you suggest -- following up in the editorial or Op-Ed sections, or simply asking the readers -- all lack practicality.  My suggestion would be for The Times (and other news media) to maintain a chart, or a list, of unresolved issues.  Ideally, it would be accessible from day to day on the Web, and perhaps printed periodically in the newspaper.  The chart could be ordered either chronologically or by priority as defined by the newspaper's editors. It could refer to the issue, the first article published on the topic and any subsequent follow-ups.  - JOHN HAWKINSON  Cambridge, Mass.  You suggest that newspapers ask readers what they want to know more about.  For The New York Times, whose readers are already well served, this question may suffice.  But for many Americans in markets with only one daily newspaper, the substantive issue is the newspaper's civic responsibility to serve as watchdog and deliver all of the news that affects people's lives.  For these people, the newspapers should be asking what the readers need to know.  - RALPH GROSS  Des Moines, Iowa </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20040828.0077 type=story > <HEADLINE> NYTR-CHEAP-GUNS-REFUSED </HEADLINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_200408.gz"

, they said,	 The eyes of someone you kill are immortal, if they face you at the fatal instant. They shake you more than the streams of blood and the death rattles, even in a great turmoil of dying. The man who says this is a mass murderer, and his eerie eloquence catches you off guard. If you've seen the film Hotel Rwanda, think of this as the other side of the story. Hatzfeld interviewed 10 men who helped slaughter 800,000 of their fellow Rwandans in a mere three months in 1994. They were in prison when Hatzfeld saw them. Most are now free. They describe their actions with such lyrical nonchalance that they inspire in a reader not horror at the violence, but something worse: a clear feeling that the potential for such behavior is not an aberration, but a willingness that is far more common among human beings. In her preface, the late Susan Sontag says that everyone should read this book in order to understand what happened in Rwanda. The killers, like others before them, followed orders. Afterward, they went home to their families. They held their children. By Alan Cowell.  KURDS-MISCREANTS (Erbil, Iraq) -- Each night at 10 p.m., all eyes in Kurdistan have been on a sadistic gay sheik and a band of miscreants that he says he led through a series of crimes so grotesque that people speak of them with their voices lowered. By James Glanz.  MEXICO-NEWSPAPER (Oaxaca, Mexico) -- For 28 days, a couple of dozen editors, reporters and other employees of a newspaper here have been besieged in their newsroom as the result of a labor dispute with a union that has close ties to the government and claims to represent the newspaper's workers. By James C. McKinley Jr.  CHINA-OIL-STRUGGLE (Jingbian, China) -- A report on what has been called one of the most important legal battles ever fought in China, as small- and medium-size oil investors have banded together in a class-action effort to push the Communist government as never before to respect its own rules. By Howard W. French.  NATIONAL  (Coded a)  TEXAS-CHURCH (Houston) -- A visit to Lakewood Church, which boasts the largest congregation in the United States and a pastor who has become a global star among televangelists. With the governor on hand and thousands in the pews, Lakewood opened a new sanctuary this weekend: an arena that was once the home for the Houston Rockets of the National Basketball Association. The church, which was established in 1959 in an abandoned feed store, recently became the first in the United States to average more than 30,000 worshippers weekly. By John Leland.  IRAQ-BOY (Washington) -- Ayad Al-Sirowiy came to America this week hoping doctors here could remove the war embedded in his face. Thirteen years old, small and skinny, Ayad was severely burned and blinded in one eye when a cluster bomb blew up in his face at the beginning of the Iraq war. By Jeffrey Gettleman.  WASHINGTON  (Coded w)  CIA-LEAK-LAW (Undated) -- A look at the law that is at the bottom of the Rove controversy. By Adam Liptak.  WHITEHOUSE-LETTER (Washington) --When Laura Bush said in a television interview last week that she hoped her husband would name a woman to replace the retiring Justice Sandra Day O'Connor on the Supreme Court, a lot of people saw it as a top item on the first lady's honey-do list. But Republicans close to the White House said that people had it reversed. Mrs. Bush, they said, was not so much nudging her husband as reflecting his thinking. By Elisabeth Bumiller.  TERROR-FBI (Washington) -- On an FBI terrorist surveillance program that rights advocates say threatens to clamp down on their rights to protest Bush administration policies. By Eric Lichtblau.  FINANCIAL  (Coded f)  ONLINE-MAPS (San Francisco) -- In the last few weeks, both Google and Yahoo published documentation making it significantly easier for programmers to link virtually any kind of Internet data to Web-based maps and, in Google's case, satellite imagery. Later this summer, Microsoft plans to introduce a competing service, Virtual Earth, with software that programmers will be able to use in similarly creative ways. So far the uses have been noncommercial. But Yahoo, Google and Microsoft are creating the services with the expectation that they will become a focal point in one of the next significant growth areas in Internet advertising: contextual advertisements tied to specific locations. By John Markoff.  HOLLYWOOD-CHRISTIANS (Los Angeles) -- Mainstream Hollywood, after decades of ignoring the pious, or occasionally defying them, is adjusting to what it perceives to be a rising religiosity in American culture. Producers, directors, studio executives and marketing specialists have been looking to either mollify or entice an audience that made its power felt with last year's Passion of the Christ. That film, along with the empowerment of a Christian conservative bloc after the last presidential election, helped change attitudes and practices in an industry usually known for its secularism. By Sharon Waxman.  MARTHA-COMEBACK (Undated) -- By attempting a corporate comeback while still serving out her felony sentence, Martha Stewart is in uncharted territory. And so, it appears, is her company's stock. While many media executives are lamenting investors' lack of enthusiasm for their companies' shares, Martha Stewart Living Omnimedia has been on a tear. Its stock price has nearly tripled in the last year, a period during which the Standard &amp; Poor's media index has gone sideways. By Richard Siklos.  CARR-COLUMN (Undated) -- Two strivers came together in a room in July of 2003. One was a former body builder turned actor who wanted to be king, at least of California, and the other was a former CPA who has refashioned himself into a media mogul. Each had something the other wanted, although it could be argued that David Pecker, the chief executive of American Media Inc. needed Arnold Schwarzenegger more. The weekly media column, by David Carr.  E-COMMERCE-COLUMN (Undated) -- After giving its Internet competitors a huge head start, Clear Channel is ready to play catch-up. The radio industry's dominant company, with more than 1,200 stations, has begun introducing its first meaningful online strategy. By Bob Tedeschi.  TERROR-FICTION (Undated) -- The author of Incendiary, a novel about a fictitious London bombing that was released July 7, goes online to ask what he should do with his book now. By Tom Zeller Jr.  POTTER-PUBLISHER (Paris) -- This is the magical summer season when Hogwarts students loll on vacation and Bloomsbury Publishing's chief executive, Nigel Newton, hunkers down in his 18th-century Soho Square offices in London to monitor sales of the sixth installment of the Harry Potter epic. By the end of this year, analysts expect that Bloomsbury will have amassed sizable resources to allow the publishing house to press onward with its global expansion and to search for new writers. By Doreen Carvajal.  DATA-AD-COLUMN (Undated) -- Over the last few years, financial services and technology companies have been quietly tweaking their advertising to incorporate themes about the safety of customers' data. But in the wake of recent crises, that message has shifted from the background to center stage. By Eric Dash.  GLF-WIE-MARKETING (Undated) -- Michelle Wie came in 23rd at the U.S. Women's Open last month, behind other personable young golfers. Wie failed to survive elimination in the PGA Tour's John Deere Classic this month and fell short in her attempt to qualify for the Masters tournament next year. Yet Wie, 15, is the golfer that sports marketers have their eyes on. By Louise Story.  CYC-VECSEY-COLUMN (St. Lary-Soulan, France) -- Sports of The Times column off the Tour de France, Stage 15, Lezat-sur-Leze to St.-Lary-Soulan. By George Vecsey.  GLF-ANDERSON-COLUMN (St. Andrews, Scotland) -- Sports of The Times column on the British Open. By Dave Anderson.  BOX-HOPKINS-TAYLOR (Las Vegas) -- Follow-up on Saturday's Hopkins-Taylor fight. By Richard Sandomir.  BBO-WOOD-BATS (Marietta, Ga.) -- When professional scouts watch young players at the plate, the kind of being bat being used can make all the difference in how they make their evaluations. By Ray Glier.  CHINA-ARTS (Washington) -- As it races toward a more market-driven economy, China is building new theaters, concert halls and museums nearly as fast as it is erecting Western-style shopping malls, hotels and business complexes. But after a half century of the arts being supported exclusively by the government, many arts organizations are on their own to develop financing, programming and audiences. A cadre of nascent Chinese arts executives spent the past two weeks at the Kennedy Center, getting an intense immersion in how the nonprofit arts operate. By Lynette Clemetson.  SMITHS-MUSICAL-REVIEW (London) -- The Smiths -- a band from Manchester, England, whose singer and lyricist, Morrissey, taught a generation-wide cult how to mope with melodramatic self-consciousness -- are getting a West End treatment in Some Girls Are Bigger Than Others, a music-theater piece based on Smiths songs. The Smiths' lyrics were proudly defenseless and unguarded, yet the staging doesn't wrap them in obvious scenarios. The show is an allusive, surreal, ever-mutating fantasia on love and sex, family and control, violence and death. By Jon Pareles.  MCCARTHY-BOOK-REVIEW (Undated) -- No Country for Old Men, by Cormac McCarthy. Reviewed by Michiko Kakutani.  SOMERS-THEATER-REVIEW (New York) -- The Blonde in the Thunderbird, a solo show starring Suzanne Somers. Reviewed by Charles Isherwood.  CD-REVIEWS (Undated) -- New hip-hop CDs from Slim Thug, Webbie and the Diplomats. Reviewed by Kelefa Sanneh.  FRANCE-OPERA-FEST (Aix-en-Provence, France) -- Before this month's Aix-en-Provence Festival, the buzz centered on Patrice Chereau's Cosi Fan Tutte, his first venture into opera in 11 years. Mozart's works have been the focus of this festival since it was founded in the late 1940s, and usually two of his operas are presented each season in the open-air courtyard of the former Archbishop's Palace here. This year, the other is La Clemenza di Tito, directed by Lukas Hemleb, with Paul Daniel conducting the Mahler Chamber Orchestra. By Alan Riding.
